Motion as Imaginary Extension: The Drop and Flame
Defines motion as imaginary extension via examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how motion in the sense of continuous traversal is perceived as an imaginary extension through the combination of successive images in the imagination.

Core Idea
Motion as continuous traversal is an imaginary construct, not directly sensed.

This lesson introduces a key concept in Islamic cosmology: motion (حركة) understood as a continuous line of movement is not a direct sensory object but rather a construction of the imagination. The author uses two vivid examples—a falling drop and a rotating flame—to show how the common sense (الحس المشترك) combines discrete images into a continuous extended impression. Understanding this imaginary nature is essential for later arguments about time.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without overthinking. Then read the translation. Notice the examples of the falling drop and rotating flame.
The passage begins with the heading of the Fourth Observation, Third Intent.
2
Understand the two images phenomenon
The author says two successive images combine in the imagination, making the mind perceive them as one extended thing. Relate this to the examples: a falling drop appears as a line; a rotating flame appears as a circle.
This is an error of the senses discussed earlier in the book.
3
Distinguish between common sense and imagination
The author notes that motion in the sense of traversal is not visible in the common sense because the image combination happens in imagination only, not in the common sense. This is why the drop and flame are seen as lines/circles, but motion itself is not directly seen.
Common sense (الحس المشترك) is the faculty that integrates sensory data.
4
Reflect on the imaginary nature
The key takeaway: motion as a continuous extended thing exists only in imagination (وهم). This sets the stage for the next lesson on time.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الرابع - المقصد الثالث : وقع الحركة من المقولات وتغيراتها فقد اجتمعت الصورتان في الخيال فيشعر الذهن بالصورتين معاً على أنهما شيء واحد ممتد (كما يحصل من القطرة النازلة والشعلة المدارة ( أمر ممتد ( في الحس المشترك فيرى ) لذلك ) خطأ أو دائرة ( كما مر في صدر الكتاب في مباحث أغلاط الحس، وإنما لم تكن الحركة بمعنى القطع مرئية مثلهما لأنَّ اجتماع الصور فيها إنما هو في الخيال لا في الحس المشترك ) وأنت تعلم من (هذا الذي ذكرناه في تحقيق الحركة معنى القطع وتصويرها أنّ قبولها للزيادة والنقصان والتقدم والانقسام لا يمنع أن يكون ) هي أمراً ( وهمياً ) لأنَّ قبولها لهذه الأمور، إنما هو بحسب التوهم، فإنَّ الأمر الممتد الموهوم يتصف بها قطعاً ( فلا يتم دليل إثبات الزمان ) وذلك إما لأنَّ العمدة في إثباته قبوله للزيادة والنقصان والتقدر والانقسام كما مر ويجوز أن يكون قبوله لها في التوهم فقط وذلك لا يمنع كونه أمراً ،وهمياً، وإما لأنَّ الزمان مقدار الحركة بمعنى القطع على المذهب المختار عندهم، فإذا لم يكن لهذه الحركة وجود لم يكن لمقدارها أيضاً ،وجود فيكون هذا معارضاً لأدلة وجوده ده فلا يترتب عليها مدلولها، وهو المراد بعدم تمامها وقد سلف منافي مباحث الزمان تحقيق أن الموجود من الحركة والزمان أمر لا ينقسم في امتداد المسافة وأنهما يرسمان في الخيال الحركة والزمان المنقسمين في ذلك الامتداد فارجع إليه .
The Fourth Observation - The Third Intent: The occurrence of motion among categories and its changes. The two images have combined in the imagination, so the mind perceives both images together as one extended thing (as happens from the falling drop and the rotating flame (an extended thing (in the common sense, so it sees) therefore) a line or a circle (as was mentioned in the beginning of the book in the investigations of errors of the senses, and motion in the sense of traversal was not visible like them only because the combination of images in it is only in the imagination, not in the common sense). And you know from (this which we mentioned in the investigation of motion in the sense of traversal and its depiction that its acceptance of increase and decrease and precedence and division does not prevent it from being) an imaginary thing) because its acceptance of these matters is only according to imagination, for the imagined extended thing is necessarily characterized by them. (Thus the proof of time is not established) either because the mainstay in its proof is its acceptance of increase and decrease and divisibility as was mentioned, and it is possible that its acceptance of these is only in imagination, and that does not prevent it from being an imaginary thing; or because time is the measure of motion in the sense of traversal according to the chosen doctrine among them, so if this motion has no existence, its measure also has no existence, so this opposes the proofs of its existence, so they do not yield their implication, and that is meant by its not being complete. And it has already preceded in the discussions of time the verification that what exists of motion and time is something indivisible in the extension of distance, and that they delineate in the imagination the motion and time that are divided in that extension, so refer to it.
Source pages p. 212

Commentary
This passage begins with the heading of the fourth observation and third intent regarding the occurrence of motion in categories. The author then explains that motion as a continuous traversal (الحركة بمعنى القطع) is an imaginary extension. Two successive images combine in the imagination, giving the impression of a continuous extended thing, like a falling drop or a rotating flame appearing as a line or circle. This is a result of the common sense (الحس المشترك) perceiving the images continuously, but actually the images are sequential. The author then says that even though this motion is imaginary, it still accepts increase, decrease, precedence, and division, but these properties are also according to imagination. This leads to the consequence that the proof of time (دليل إثبات الزمان) is not complete. The proof relies on time's divisibility and extension, but since motion (its measure) is imaginary, time's divisibility could be merely imaginary, and if motion has no real existence, its measure (time) also lacks real existence. Therefore, the proofs for time's existence are opposed and do not yield their conclusions. The author concludes by referencing earlier discussions on time where it was verified that the existent from motion and time is indivisible in the extension of distance, and they are depicted in imagination as divided.

Key Terms
الحركة بمعنى القطع
التوهم
الإمتداد
الحس المشترك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the author say motion in the sense of traversal is not visible like the line or circle from the drop and flame?
A) Because motion is too fast to be seen.
B) Because the image combination for motion occurs only in imagination, not in common sense.
C) Because motion is actually a sensory illusion.
D) Because the drop and flame are not examples of motion.

Key Takeaway
Motion as continuous traversal is an imaginary extension formed by combining successive images in the imagination, not a direct object of the common sense.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage teaches that what you perceive as continuous motion is actually a construction of your imagination. Where in your life have you mistaken an imaginary construct for a direct reality? Will you examine such assumptions today?
?
If your senses can be deceived by the mere combination of images, how can you ensure that your beliefs about Allah—especially matters not seen by the eye—are based on truth rather than imagination? What step will you take to distinguish between them?
?
The example of the rotating flame shows that a series of still positions can create the illusion of a circle. In your spiritual life, are there 'circles' you take for granted that might actually be illusions of continuity? Are you willing to pause and see the discrete moments?

Why the Proof of Time Fails
Argues that the proof of time fails due to the imaginary nature of motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two reasons why the philosophical proof for time's existence is undermined if motion is merely imaginary.

Core Idea
If motion is imaginary, time as its measure lacks real existence.

In the previous lesson, we learned that motion in the sense of traversal is an imaginary extension. Now, the author draws the theological consequence: the standard proof for the existence of time—based on time's divisibility and extension—collapses. This is because time is defined as the measure of motion, and if motion has only imaginary existence, its measure (time) also becomes imaginary. The author presents two distinct arguments to show why the proof fails, and he refers back to earlier discussions for further clarification.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the two reasons
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Look for the two 'إما لأن...' phrases. These list the two reasons why the proof of time is not complete.
The passage is compact; note the structure: 'فلا يتم دليل إثبات الزمان' then 'وذلك إما لأن... وإما لأن...'.
2
Understand the first reason
The first reason: The mainstay of proving time is its divisibility and extension. But these could be merely imaginary because motion (which time measures) is imaginary. So the proof does not establish real existence.
The author says 'يُجوز أن يكون قبوله لها في التوهم فقط'.
3
Understand the second reason
The second reason: Time is defined as the measure of motion (بمعنى القطع). If motion has no real existence, its measure (time) also has no real existence. This contradicts other proofs of time's existence, so the proof fails.
The author says 'فإذا لم يكن لهذه الحركة وجود لم يكن لمقدارها أيضاً وجود'.
4
Connect to the earlier discussion
The author concludes by referring to earlier verification that what exists of motion and time is indivisible, and the divided extension we imagine is a mental construct. Reflect on how this resolves the apparent contradiction.
This is a reference to a previous passage; you may revisit it for depth.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الرابع - المقصد الثالث : وقع الحركة من المقولات وتغيراتها فقد اجتمعت الصورتان في الخيال فيشعر الذهن بالصورتين معاً على أنهما شيء واحد ممتد (كما يحصل من القطرة النازلة والشعلة المدارة ( أمر ممتد ( في الحس المشترك فيرى ) لذلك ) خطأ أو دائرة ( كما مر في صدر الكتاب في مباحث أغلاط الحس، وإنما لم تكن الحركة بمعنى القطع مرئية مثلهما لأنَّ اجتماع الصور فيها إنما هو في الخيال لا في الحس المشترك ) وأنت تعلم من (هذا الذي ذكرناه في تحقيق الحركة معنى القطع وتصويرها أنّ قبولها للزيادة والنقصان والتقدم والانقسام لا يمنع أن يكون ) هي أمراً ( وهمياً ) لأنَّ قبولها لهذه الأمور، إنما هو بحسب التوهم، فإنَّ الأمر الممتد الموهوم يتصف بها قطعاً ( فلا يتم دليل إثبات الزمان ) وذلك إما لأنَّ العمدة في إثباته قبوله للزيادة والنقصان والتقدر والانقسام كما مر ويجوز أن يكون قبوله لها في التوهم فقط وذلك لا يمنع كونه أمراً ،وهمياً، وإما لأنَّ الزمان مقدار الحركة بمعنى القطع على المذهب المختار عندهم، فإذا لم يكن لهذه الحركة وجود لم يكن لمقدارها أيضاً ،وجود فيكون هذا معارضاً لأدلة وجوده ده فلا يترتب عليها مدلولها، وهو المراد بعدم تمامها وقد سلف منافي مباحث الزمان تحقيق أن الموجود من الحركة والزمان أمر لا ينقسم في امتداد المسافة وأنهما يرسمان في الخيال الحركة والزمان المنقسمين في ذلك الامتداد فارجع إليه .
The Fourth Observation - The Third Intent: The occurrence of motion among categories and its changes. The two images have combined in the imagination, so the mind perceives both images together as one extended thing (as happens from the falling drop and the rotating flame (an extended thing (in the common sense, so it sees) therefore) a line or a circle (as was mentioned in the beginning of the book in the investigations of errors of the senses, and motion in the sense of traversal was not visible like them only because the combination of images in it is only in the imagination, not in the common sense). And you know from (this which we mentioned in the investigation of motion in the sense of traversal and its depiction that its acceptance of increase and decrease and precedence and division does not prevent it from being) an imaginary thing) because its acceptance of these matters is only according to imagination, for the imagined extended thing is necessarily characterized by them. (Thus the proof of time is not established) either because the mainstay in its proof is its acceptance of increase and decrease and divisibility as was mentioned, and it is possible that its acceptance of these is only in imagination, and that does not prevent it from being an imaginary thing; or because time is the measure of motion in the sense of traversal according to the chosen doctrine among them, so if this motion has no existence, its measure also has no existence, so this opposes the proofs of its existence, so they do not yield their implication, and that is meant by its not being complete. Commentary
This passage begins with the heading of the fourth observation and third intent regarding the occurrence of motion in categories. The author then explains that motion as a continuous traversal (الحركة بمعنى القطع) is an imaginary extension. Two successive images combine in the imagination, giving the impression of a continuous extended thing, like a falling drop or a rotating flame appearing as a line or circle. Therefore, the proofs for time's existence are opposed and do not yield their conclusions. The author concludes by referencing earlier discussions on time where it was verified that the existent from motion and time is indivisible in the extension of distance, and they are depicted in imagination as divided.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that if time appears divisible and extended, it must be a real thing.
Correction: The author argues that divisibility and extension can be merely imaginary, as in the case of motion, so these properties do not guarantee real existence.

Checkpoint
What are the two reasons given for why the proof of time fails?
A) Time cannot be measured and motion is non-existent.
B) Divisibility may be imaginary and time is the measure of motion which is imaginary.
C) Time is eternal and motion is created.
D) The proof relies on common sense, which is fallible.

Key Takeaway
The proof of time fails because its reliance on divisibility and on time as the measure of motion leads to circularity when motion is imaginary.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your life's motion is an imaginary extension, and time is merely its measure, what distinguishes a real deed from an imagined one? Will you examine your actions today to see which are truly moving you towards Allah, not just passing time?
?
The proof of time fails because it relies on imaginary attributes. Could your own spiritual proofs be similarly flawed? Are you certain your faith rests on what is real, or have you accepted comfortable illusions? Commit to verifying one foundational belief this week.
?
You live as though time is an unstoppable force. But this passage suggests time has no independent reality. How should this change your urgency? If your movement towards Allah is what truly matters, will you sacrifice one hour of 'time' today for an action that leaves a lasting trace in your soul?

Correct Meaning of Motion in Categories and Introduction to the Four Categories
The author states the correct definition and lists the four categories in which motion occurs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the correct definition of motion in categories and know the four categories in which motion occurs, beginning with quantity.

Core Idea
Motion in categories means movement between species, varieties, or individuals of that category.

This lesson clarifies a key concept in Islamic philosophy: what it truly means for motion to occur within a category. The author begins by correcting a common misunderstanding and then defines the precise nature of such motion. He then lists the four categories where motion is found, starting with quantity and its two fundamental directions.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of motion in categories
Read the Arabic passage once. Notice the three levels: species (نوع), variety (صنف), and individual (فرد). The subject moves from one of these within the same category to another.
2
Identify the four categories
The passage states that motion occurs in four categories. Although only the first (quantity) is elaborated here, the complete list is well-known: quantity, quality, posture, and location.
This answers the misconception that motion occurs in all ten categories.
3
Focus on the first category: quantity
Motion in quantity has two aspects: increase and decrease. Read the explanation carefully.
4
Reflect on the implications
Think about why motion is restricted to these four categories. The commentary will help you understand the reasoning behind this classification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والصواب أنَّ معنى وقوعها فيها هو أنَّ الموضوع يتحرك من نوع لتلك المقولة إلى نوع آخر منها أو من صنف إلى صنف أو من فرد إلى فرد (وهي ( أي المقولات التي تقع فيها الحركة ( أربع : ( كما هو المشهور ( الأولى الكم (وهو) أي وقوع الحركة فيه ( على أربعة أوجه ) لأنَّ الحركة في الكم إما بطريق الازدياد أو بطريق الانتقاص،
And the correct [view] is that the meaning of its occurrence in them is that the subject moves from a species of that category to another species of it, or from a variety to a variety, or from an individual to an individual. And they (i.e., the categories in which motion occurs) are four: as is well-known. The first is quantity; and it (i.e., the occurrence of motion in it) is in four aspects, because motion in quantity is either by way of increase or by way of decrease.
Source pages p. 213

Commentary
This passage gives the correct definition of motion occurring in categories. The author states that the meaning is that the subject (الموضوع) moves from one species (نوع) of that category to another species, or from one variety (صنف) to another, or from one individual (فرد) to another. Then he enumerates the four categories in which motion occurs, starting with quantity (الكم). Motion in quantity has four aspects: increase and decrease. The text begins the enumeration but is cut off at the page end. The author follows a classification common in Islamic philosophy: motion occurs in the categories of quantity, quality, posture, and location (الكم، الكيف، الوضع، الأين). The passage here only begins to explain quantity.

Key Terms
الموضوع
النوع
الصنف
الفرد
الكم
الازدياد
الانتقاص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Motion occurs in all ten Aristotelian categories.
Correction: The correct view is that motion occurs only in four categories: quantity, quality, posture, and location. The other six categories (substance, relation, time, position, state, action, and passion) do not admit motion.

Checkpoint
According to the correct view, what does motion in a category mean?
A) The category itself changes gradually.
B) The subject moves from one species, variety, or individual of that category to another.
C) The category is the genus of motion.
D) The category remains constant while the quality changes.

Key Takeaway
Motion in categories is defined as the subject moving between types within that category, and it occurs only in four categories, starting with quantity where it takes the forms of increase and decrease.

Three Meanings of Rarefaction and Condensation
Al-Jurjani classifies takhallul and takathuf into three meanings across categories.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that rarefaction (takhallul) and condensation (takathuf) each have three distinct meanings: one as motion in the category of quantity, one as a change in position (inflation/contraction), and one as a quality (thinness/thickness).

Core Idea
Rarefaction and condensation each have three meanings across quantity, quality, and position.

Al-Jurjani here clarifies that the terms takhallul and takathuf are not univocal. They can refer to changes in volume (motion in quantity), changes in the arrangement of parts (position), or the resulting thinness/thickness (quality). This classification prevents confusion when reading kalam physics.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
First read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice the technical terms: التخلخل، التكاثف، الانفشاش، الاندماج، الرقة، الثخانة.
2
Identify the three categories
The author states that each term has three meanings: one in the category of quantity (the original change in volume), one in position (inflation and contraction), and one in quality (thinness and thickness). Find where he mentions each.
3
Note the homonymy
Al-Jurjani warns that the names are applied to different phenomena by homonymy (اشتراك اللفظ). Inflation and contraction are not the same as rarefaction and condensation proper; they belong to the category of position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه ( الثاني : التكاثف وهو ضد التخلخل يعني أنه انتقاص حجم الجسم من غير أن ينفصل عنه جزء، وقد مر ما يدل على آنيته ولميته ) واعلم أنهما ) أن أي التخلخل والتكاثف المذكورين في الحركة الكمية (غير الانفشاش وهو تتباعد الأجزاء) بعضها عن بعض ( ويدخلها الهواء ) أو جسم آخر غريب كالقطن المنفوش ) وغير الاندماج وهو ضده ) فهو أن تتقارب الأجزاء الوحدانية الطبع بحيث يخرج عنها ما بينها من الجسم الغريب كالقطن الملفوف بعد نفشه ( وإن كان يطلق عليهما الاسم أي يطلق اسم التخلخل على الانفشاش واسم التكاثف على الاندماج ( باشتراك اللفظ فإنّ هذين) أي الانفشاش والاندماج (من مقولة الوضع) فإن الأجزاء بسبب حركتها الأينية إلى التباعد والتقارب تحصل لها هيئة باعتبار نسب بعضها إلى بعض ( وقد يطلق ) اسم التخلخل ) على الرقة ) أي رقة القوام (و) اسم التكاثف ( على الثخانة وهو ( أي المذكور أعني التخلخل والتكاثف بمعنى الرقة والثخانة ( من باب الكيف فلكل واحد منهما ثلاثة معان: اثنان منها من مقولتي الكيف والوضع، وواحد منها حركة في مقولة الكم .
The second aspect: condensation, which is the opposite of rarefaction, meaning that it is a decrease in the volume of a body without a part separating from it, and what indicates its quiddity and cause has passed. And know that they (i.e., rarefaction and condensation mentioned in quantitative motion) are other than inflation, which is the separation of parts from each other and air enters them, or another foreign body like fluffed cotton, and other than contraction, which is its opposite; it is that the simple natural parts draw near such that the foreign body between them exits, like rolled cotton after its fluffing. And the name is applied to them, i.e., the name rarefaction is applied to inflation and the name condensation to contraction, by homonymy. For these two (i.e., inflation and contraction) belong to the category of position, because the parts, due to their locative motion towards separation and convergence, acquire a configuration in consideration of their relations to one another. And the name rarefaction may be applied to thinness, i.e., delicacy of consistency, and the name condensation to thickness, and that (i.e., rarefaction and condensation in the sense of thinness and thickness) is from the category of quality. So each of them has three meanings: two of them from the categories of quality and position, and one of them is a motion in the category of quantity.
Source pages p. 216

Commentary
This passage is a classification of the concepts of rarefaction (takhallul) and condensation (takathuf). The author first defines condensation as a decrease in volume without loss of parts, recalling that prior evidence established its existence and cause. He then distinguishes these from two other phenomena: inflation (infishash), where parts separate and air enters, and contraction (indimaj), where parts converge and expel foreign substances. Although the same Arabic terms are sometimes used for these different phenomena by homonymy, inflation and contraction belong to the category of position (wad'), because parts move to change their relative positions. Additionally, rarefaction and condensation can refer to thinness (riqqa) and thickness (thikhana) in quality (kayf). The key outcome is that each of the original terms has three distinct meanings: one as motion in quantity (the original change in volume), one as a positional change, and one as a quality.

Key Terms
التخلخل
التكاثف
الانفشاش
الاندماج
الرقة
الثخانة
مقولة الكم
مقولة الكيف
مقولة الوضع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Rarefaction and condensation are the same as inflation and contraction.
Correction: Inflation and contraction are distinct phenomena that belong to the category of position, not quantity. The same names are only applied by homonymy.

Checkpoint
How many distinct meanings does the author identify for each of takhallul and takathuf?
A) Two meanings: one in quantity and one in quality.
B) Three meanings: in quantity, quality, and position.
C) Three meanings: in quantity, position, and substance.
D) Two meanings: in quantity and position.

Key Takeaway
Rarefaction and condensation are polysemous: they refer to quantitative change, positional change, and qualitative state.

Defining Natural Growth: Distinction from Fatness and Swelling
Al-Jurjani defines natural growth and distinguishes it from fatness and swelling.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise definition of natural growth (النمو) and how it differs from fatness (السمن) and swelling (الورم) according to the classical kalam tradition.

Core Idea
Growth increases all dimensions naturally; fatness does not, and swelling is unnatural.

This passage from al-Jurjani's Sharh al-Mawaqif sets out the technical definition of growth (النمو) as a natural increase in volume in all dimensions. It then distinguishes growth from fatness and swelling by examining two key criteria: coverage of all dimensions and natural proportionality. Understanding this distinction is important for kalam discussions about bodily change, resurrection, and the nature of living beings.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of growth
Read the Arabic text focusing on the definition of growth (النمو). Note the three conditions: increase in volume, in all dimensions, and natural proportion.
The term 'أقطار' means dimensions: length, width, depth.
2
Compare growth with fatness
The passage argues that fatness does not increase length (manifestly), so it fails the 'all dimensions' condition. Even if imperceptible increase occurs, it does not affect original parts.
The commentator clarifies that the increase in length from fatness is real but imperceptible.
3
Compare growth with swelling
Swelling lacks natural proportionality, so it is not growth. Growth is proportional and natural.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه ( الثالث : النمو وهو ازدياد حجم الجسم بما ينضم إليه ويداخله في جميع الأقطار بنسبة طبيعية بخلاف السمن والورم ) أما السمن فإنه على ما قيل ليس في جميع الأقطار إذ لا يزداد به الطول، وأما الورم فليس على نسبة طبيعية. قوله : ( على ما قيل إلخ ) إشارة إلى أنَّ الزيادة في الطول متحققة إلا أنها غير محسوسة. قوله : (لا يزداد به الطول ( أي زيادة ظاهرة كزيادة العرض والعمق، وإلا فالسمن يزيد جانب الرأس والقدم قابلا غير محسوس وبعضهم قالوا : المراد زيادة في جميع أقطار الأجزاء الأصلية، والسمن لا يزيد في أقطار الأجزاء الأصلية ولذا يجتمع النمو الهزال لكن على هذا مع يلزم استدراك اعتبار قيد الجمع إذ السمن لا يزيد في قطرين بل أقطار الأجزاء الأصلية.
The third aspect: growth, which is the increase of the body's volume by what joins it and enters it in all dimensions in a natural proportion, unlike fatness and swelling. As for fatness, according to what has been said, it is not in all dimensions, because length does not increase by it. As for swelling, it is not in a natural proportion. His statement: 'according to what has been said, etc.' is an indication that the increase in length is realized but it is imperceptible. His statement: 'length does not increase by it' means a manifest increase like the increase in width and depth; otherwise, fatness increases the side of the head and foot imperceptibly. And some said: what is meant is increase in all dimensions of the original parts, and fatness does not increase in the dimensions of the original parts, and therefore growth and emaciation coexist. But on this, a qualification of the condition of universality must be supplied, because fatness does not increase in just two dimensions but in the dimensions of the original parts.
Source pages p. 216

Commentary
This passage defines growth (namuww) and distinguishes it from fatness (saman) and swelling (waram). The author states that growth is an increase in volume in all dimensions (aqtar) naturally, i.e., proportionally. Fatness does not increase length (asperceptibly), and swelling lacks natural proportionality. The commentator then clarifies that the increase in length from fatness is real but imperceptible, and some argue that the condition of 'all dimensions' applies specifically to the original parts (al-ajza' al-asliyya), such as bones, which do not increase in fatness, allowing growth and emaciation to coexist. Thus, fatness fails the condition of increasing all original parts in all dimensions.

Key Terms
النمو
السمن
الورم
الأقطار
الأجزاء الأصلية
الهزال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that fatness increases all dimensions, but the author clarifies that length does not increase perceptibly, and even imperceptibly, the original parts do not increase.
Correction: Fatness does not meet the condition of increasing all dimensions because length does not increase manifestly, and the original parts (like bones) remain unchanged.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what distinguishes growth from fatness?
A) Growth occurs only in young animals, while fatness occurs in old ones.
B) Growth increases all dimensions naturally, while fatness does not increase length manifestly.
C) Growth is a motion in quantity, while fatness is a motion in quality.
D) Growth is voluntary, while fatness is involuntary.

Key Takeaway
Growth is a natural increase in volume in all dimensions, unlike fatness which does not increase length manifestly, and swelling which lacks natural proportion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You just learned that growth is an increase in all dimensions in natural proportion. In your own life, are you growing in all spiritual dimensions—faith, character, knowledge, action—or only in some areas while others remain stagnant? Which dimension have you neglected, and what will you do this week to address it?
?
Fatness increases volume but not length. Are you pursuing spiritual 'growth' that is merely superficial—more activities, more knowledge—but not deepening your connection with Allah or transforming your character? What specific change would be a true increase in 'length' for you?
?
Swelling is unnatural and disproportionate. Is there any area of your life where your growth is forced, imbalanced, or driven by ego rather than by the natural order Allah placed? How can you realign with that natural proportion today?

Defining Wilting and Distinguishing It from Fatness and Emaciation
Al-Jurjani defines wilting and presents Imam Razi's clarification of its distinction from fatness/emaciation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise definition of wilting as the opposite of growth, and how Imam al-Razi distinguishes it from fatness and emaciation based on the behavior of original and accessory parts.

Core Idea
Wilting is a natural decrease in volume across all dimensions, not just weight loss.

Imam al-Razi begins by clarifying a common confusion: growth and wilting are often mixed up with fatness and emaciation. He then provides a detailed explanation of how growth actually occurs through the assimilation of new parts into the original ones, which sets the stage for understanding its opposite—wilting.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of wilting
Find the sentence that defines wilting as the opposite of growth. Notice that it describes a decrease in volume in all dimensions in a natural proportion.
Wilting is not simply shrinking; it is a systematic reduction.
2
Distinguish wilting from fatness/emaciation
Identify how Imam al-Razi differentiates these concepts. Growth and wilting are about the movement of original organs, while fatness and emaciation affect only the flesh.
A person can be growing yet emaciated, or stopped growing yet fat.
3
Understand the process of growth
Study the description of growth: new parts (accessory parts) enter pores of original parts, assimilate, and push the original parts outward in all dimensions. This is the benchmark for understanding wilting as its reverse.
This mechanism explains why growth is a quantitative motion for the original parts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه (الرابع: الذبول ) وهو (عكسه ) أي عكس النمو فهو انتقاص حجم الجسم بسبب ما ينفصل عنه في جميع الأقطار على نسبة طبيعية، قال الإمام الرازي : قد يشتبه النمو والذبول بالسمن والهزال والفرق أنَّ الواقف في النمو قد يسمن كما أن المتزايد في النمو قد يهزل وتحقيقه أن الزيادة إذا أحدثت المنافذ في الأجزاء الأصلية ودخلت فيها وتشبهت بطبيعتها واندفعت الأجزاء الأصلية إلى جميع الأقطار على نسبة واحدة مناسبة لطبيعة النوع فذلك النمو، وأما الشيخ إذا صار سميناً فإنَّ أجزاءه الأصلية قد جفت وصلبت فلا يقوى الغذاء على تفريقها والنفوذ فيها، فلذلك لا تتحرك أعضاؤه الأصلية إلى الزيادة، فلا يكون نامياً لكن لحمه يتحرك إلى الزيادة، فيكون ذلك نمواً في اللحم، إلا أنَّ اسم النمو مخصوص بحركة الأعضاء الأصلية قال : والمشهور أنّ النمو والذبول من الحركات الكمية وهو بعيد عندي فإن الأجزاء الأصلية والزائدة في المغتذى باق كل واحد منها على مقداره الذي كان عليه، نعم ربما تحرك كل واحد منها في أينه أو وضعه أو كيفه، لكن ذلك ليس حركة في الكم وقد أجيب عنه بأنَّ الأجزاء الأصلية زاد مقدارها عند النمو على ما كانت عليه قبل ذلك ضرورة دخول الأجزاء الزائدة في منافذها، وتشبهها بها ونقص مقدارها عند الذبول عما كانت عليه ،قبله، وإنكار هذا مكابرة أقول إن كان اتصال الزائدة بعد المداخلة بالأصلية على وجه يصير به المجموع متصلاً واحداً في نفسه، فالصواب ما قاله المجيب، فالقول ما قاله الإمام واعلم أنه إذا كان كذلك ...
The fourth aspect: wilting, which is its opposite, i.e., the opposite of growth. So it is a decrease of the body's volume due to what separates from it in all dimensions in a natural proportion. Imam al-Razi said: Growth and wilting may be confused with fatness and emaciation. The difference is that one who stops growing may become fat, just as one who is increasing in growth may become emaciated. The verification is that when the increase creates pores in the original parts and enters them and becomes assimilated to their nature, and the original parts are pushed in all dimensions in one proportion suitable to the nature of the species, that is growth. As for the old man when he becomes fat, his original parts have dried and hardened, so nutrition cannot separate them and penetrate them. Therefore, his original organs do not move to increase, so he is not growing; but his flesh moves to increase, so that is growth in the flesh, except that the name 'growth' is specific to the motion of the original organs. He said: The well-known view is that growth and wilting are quantitative motions, but in my view it is far-fetched, because the original and accessory parts in the nourished body each remain at their original quantity. They may move in their 'where', position, or quality, but that is not a motion in quantity. He was answered: The original parts increase in quantity at growth compared to what they were before, necessarily because the accessory parts enter their pores and assimilate to them, and decrease in quantity at wilting compared to what they were before. Denying this is obstinacy. I say: If the connection of the accessory parts after entering the original parts makes the whole a single continuous entity in itself, then what the answerer said is correct; otherwise, the statement is what the Imam said. And know that if it is so...
Source pages p. 217

Commentary
This passage covers the definition of wilting (dhubul) as the natural decrease in volume in all dimensions, opposite to growth. It then presents Imam al-Razi's objection to the common view that growth and wilting are quantitative motions (haraka fi al-kamm). Al-Razi argues that the original and accessory parts each retain their own magnitude; they may move in location, position, or quality, but not in quantity. The response affirms that the original parts do increase in size when accessory parts enter and assimilate, and decrease in wilting. The commentator (possibly al-Jurjani) then notes that if the connection makes a true unity, the response stands; otherwise, al-Razi's view holds. This is a classic kalam debate on the nature of physical change, with the final position that in true unification, quantitative change occurs in the original parts.

Key Terms
الذبول
النمو
السمن
الهزال
الأجزاء الأصلية
الأجزاء الزائدة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Wilting is the same as becoming emaciated (huzal).
Correction: Wilting specifically refers to decrease in original organs, while emaciation is loss of flesh. They are distinct concepts.

Checkpoint
According to Imam al-Razi, what distinguishes growth from fatness?
A) Growth involves the original organs increasing, while fatness is only increase in flesh.
B) Growth occurs in all dimensions, while fatness occurs only in length.
C) Growth is a quantitative motion, while fatness is qualitative.
D) Growth requires nutrition, while fatness does not.

Key Takeaway
Wilting is the opposite of growth: a natural decrease in the volume of original organs in all dimensions, distinct from mere emaciation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are there areas of your spiritual life where you have mistaken outward increase (like more knowledge or acts) for genuine growth of your heart? What would it mean for your 'original parts' to actually increase in submission to Allah?
?
Consider the image of an old man whose core has hardened while his flesh still grows. Has your heart become dry such that new good deeds don't penetrate to transform your core? What would it take to soften your original parts again?
?
If wilting of the soul is the decrease of your essential faith (iman), are you relying on the 'fatness' of outward rituals to mask a shrinking of your inner conviction? Will you measure your growth by the criteria of original organs, not just flesh?

The Debate on Whether Growth and Wilting Are Quantitative Motions
Al-Jurjani presents Imam Razi's objection to growth/wilting as quantitative motions and the response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam al-Razi's objection to classifying growth and wilting as quantitative motions, and the response that defends quantitative change by the assimilation of accessory parts into original ones.

Core Idea
Growth and wilting are quantitative because original parts themselves change size.

The well-known kalam view considers growth and wilting as quantitative motions (haraka fi al-kamm). Imam al-Razi objects, arguing that original and accessory parts each retain their own quantity. The response counters that when accessory parts assimilate, the original parts truly increase. This passage presents this debate and its resolution by the commentator.

Guided Path
1
Identify the well-known view
The passage states: 'The well-known view is that growth and wilting are quantitative motions.' Understand what this means: a change in the category of quantity (kamm).
Quantitative motion is one of the four categories of motion in kalam.
2
Study Imam al-Razi's objection
Al-Razi argues that original and accessory parts each remain at their original quantity; they only move in 'where', position, or quality. Therefore, growth is not a change in quantity.
This challenges the common classification.
3
Analyze the response
The response says that original parts do increase in quantity because accessory parts enter their pores and assimilate, becoming part of them. Denying this is obstinacy.
The key is assimilation causing a real increase in the original part's magnitude.
4
Understand the commentator's resolution
The commentator (al-Jurjani) says that if the connection makes a true continuous unity, the response is correct; otherwise, al-Razi's view stands.
This shows the importance of the nature of the connection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه (الرابع: الذبول ) وهو (عكسه ) أي عكس النمو فهو انتقاص حجم الجسم بسبب ما ينفصل عنه في جميع الأقطار على نسبة طبيعية، قال الإمام الرازي : قد يشتبه النمو والذبول بالسمن والهزال والفرق أنَّ الواقف في النمو قد يسمن كما أن المتزايد في النمو قد يهزل وتحقيقه أن الزيادة إذا أحدثت المنافذ في الأجزاء الأصلية ودخلت فيها وتشبهت بطبيعتها واندفعت الأجزاء الأصلية إلى جميع الأقطار على نسبة واحدة مناسبة لطبيعة النوع فذلك النمو، وأما الشيخ إذا صار سميناً فإنَّ أجزاءه الأصلية قد جفت وصلبت فلا يقوى الغذاء على تفريقها والنفوذ فيها، فلذلك لا تتحرك أعضاؤه الأصلية إلى الزيادة، فلا يكون نامياً لكن لحمه يتحرك إلى الزيادة، فيكون ذلك نمواً في اللحم، إلا أنَّ اسم النمو مخصوص بحركة الأعضاء الأصلية قال : والمشهور أنّ النمو والذبول من الحركات الكمية وهو بعيد عندي فإن الأجزاء الأصلية والزائدة في المغتذى باق كل واحد منها على مقداره الذي كان عليه، نعم ربما تحرك كل واحد منها في أينه أو وضعه أو كيفه، لكن ذلك ليس حركة في الكم وقد أجيب عنه بأنَّ الأجزاء الأصلية زاد مقدارها عند النمو على ما كانت عليه قبل ذلك ضرورة دخول الأجزاء الزائدة في منافذها، وتشبهها بها ونقص مقدارها عند الذبول عما كانت عليه ،قبله، وإنكار هذا مكابرة أقول إن كان اتصال الزائدة بعد المداخلة بالأصلية على وجه يصير به المجموع متصلاً واحداً في نفسه، فالصواب ما قاله المجيب، فالقول ما قاله الإمام واعلم أنه إذا كان كذلك ...

?

Growth and Withering: Definitions and Distinction from Fatness
Al-Jurjani defines withering and distinguishes growth from fatness using al-Razi's analysis.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definitions of growth (al-numu) and withering (al-dhubul) and how they differ from fatness (al-siman) and emaciation (al-huzal).

Core Idea
Growth is proportional expansion through integration of new parts; fatness is not true growth.

Before diving into the debate about motion types, al-Jurjani clarifies what growth and withering really mean. He warns against confusing these with mere fatness or thinness. In this passage, you'll see the precise conditions that make real growth distinct from simple increase in bulk.

Guided Path
1
Read the definitions of growth and withering
Start by reading the Arabic and translation for the definitions. Notice that withering is simply the reverse of growth: a decrease in all directions naturally.
Both are defined by natural proportion in all directions.
2
Understand the distinction from fatness and emaciation
Al-Razi's example of the old man shows that fatness does not change the original parts—they remain dry and hard. True growth involves the creation of pores and entry of additional parts.
Fatness is growth only in flesh, not in original parts.
3
Grasp the key condition: natural proportion
Both growth and withering must occur in all directions according to a natural proportion. Swelling (al-waram) is excluded because it lacks this proportion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الرابع - المقصد الثالث : وقع الحركة من المقولات وتغيراتها
الورم فليس على نسبة طبيعية. الوجه (الرابع: الذبول ) وهو (عكسه ) أي عكس النمو فهو انتقاص حجم الجسم بسبب ما ينفصل عنه في جميع الأقطار على نسبة طبيعية، قال الإمام الرازي : قد يشتبه النمو والذبول بالسمن والهزال والفرق أنَّ الواقف في النمو قد يسمن كما أن المتزايد في النمو قد يهزل وتحقيقه أن الزيادة إذا أحدثت المنافذ في الأجزاء الأصلية ودخلت فيها وتشبهت بطبيعتها واندفعت الأجزاء الأصلية إلى جميع الأقطار على نسبة واحدة مناسبة لطبيعة النوع فذلك النمو، وأما الشيخ إذا صار سميناً فإنَّ أجزاءه الأصلية قد جفت وصلبت فلا يقوى الغذاء على تفريقها والنفوذ فيها، فلذلك لا تتحرك أعضاؤه الأصلية إلى الزيادة، فلا يكون نامياً لكن لحمه يتحرك إلى الزيادة، فيكون ذلك نمواً في اللحم، إلا أنَّ اسم النمو مخصوص بحركة الأعضاء الأصلية قال : والمشهور أنّ النمو والذبول من الحركات الكمية وهو بعيد عندي فإن الأجزاء الأصلية والزائدة في المغتذى باق كل واحد منها على مقداره الذي كان عليه، نعم ربما تحرك كل واحد منها في أينه أو وضعه أو كيفه، لكن ذلك ليس حركة في الكم وقد أجيب عنه بأنَّ الأجزاء الأصلية زاد مقدارها عند النمو على ما كانت عليه قبل ذلك ضرورة دخول الأجزاء الزائدة في منافذها، وتشبهها بها ونقص مقدارها عند الذبول عما كانت عليه ،قبله، وإنكار هذا مكابرة أقول إن كان اتصال الزائدة بعد المداخلة بالأصلية على وجه يصير به المجموع متصلاً واحداً في نفسه، فالصواب ما قاله المجيب، فالقول ما قاله الإمام واعلم أنه إذا
قوله : ( أنَّ الزيادة ) أي الزيادة الحاصلة بسبب ورود القدر الزائد على قدر ما يتخلل.
قوله : ( إذا حدثت المنافذ بسبب كثرة الرطوبة في الجسم المعين والحرارة.
قوله: (في أينه أو وضعه أو كيفه فإنَّ الحركة الأينية تستلزم تبدل القوام لأنَّ الأجزاء القوامية تشبهت بطبيعة الأجزاء الأصلية في القوام واللون تحركت في الكيف أيضاً فكلمة أو لمنع الخلو .
قوله: (فالصواب ما قاله المجيب لأنه تحقق تغير الأجزاء الأصلية في المقدار تدريجاً والمتحرك في باقي الحالتين بشخصه بناء على أن الأجزاء الغذائية غير معتبر في التشخص، وإلا لزم انعدام الشخص آناً فاناً، فليس هذا من قبيل انضمام ماء بماء وصيرورتهما متصلاً واحداً مع
يمنع الازدياد في الأجزاء اللحمية، وحينئذ يخرج السمن بقيد الأقطار سواء اعتبر في السمن ازدياد في الطول أيضاً أم لا ، إذ ليس الازياد به في أقطار الأجزاء الأصلية أعني العظام، فتأمل.
قوله : ( فالصواب ما قاله المجيب فيه نظر لأنَّ المجموع الثاني المتقدر بعينه متقدر بمقدارين في زمانين، أو لا يرى أن الماء القليل إذا ضم إليه ماء آخر فإنهما يصيران شيئاً واحداً مع
عد النمو والذبول من الحركات الكمية، فالوجه أن يعد السمن والهزال منها أيضاً
قوله : (فالوجه أن يعد إلخ) فعده العلامة في شرح القانون منها وعبارة كتاب النجاة أيضاً تشير إلى ذلك، ولعل القوم إنما تركوا ذكرهما لأن مقصودهم بيان وقوع الأقسام الأربعة للحركة الحاصلة بالحصر العقلي في الكمية لا تعداد أفرادها .
The fourth observation - the third purpose: The occurrence of motion among the categories and their changes. Swelling is not according to a natural proportion. The fourth aspect: withering, which is its opposite, i.e., the opposite of growth, so it is a decrease in the size of the body due to what separates from it in all directions according to a natural proportion. Imam al-Razi said: Growth and withering may be confused with fatness and emaciation. The difference is that the one who stops in growth may become fat, just as the one who is increasing in growth may become emaciated. Its verification is that when the increase creates pores in the original parts, enters into them, and becomes similar to their nature, and the original parts are pushed to all directions in a single proportion suitable to the nature of the species, that is growth. As for the old man when he becomes fat, his original parts have dried and hardened, so nutrition is not able to separate them and penetrate into them. Therefore, his original organs do not move to increase, so he is not growing, but his flesh moves to increase, so that is growth in the flesh, except that the name 'growth' is specific to the movement of the original organs. He said: The well-known view is that growth and withering are among the quantitative motions, but in my view it is far-fetched, because the original and additional parts in the nourished [body] each remain on its previous magnitude. Yes, perhaps each of them moves in its 'where' or its 'position' or its 'quality', but that is not a motion in quantity. And it was answered: that the magnitude of the original parts increased at growth from what it was before by necessity of the entry of the additional parts into their pores and their similarity to them, and its magnitude decreased at withering from what it was before. Denying this is obstinacy. I say: If the connection of the additional [parts] after insertion with the original [parts] is in a manner that makes the whole a single continuous entity in itself, then what the answerer said is correct. So the statement is what the Imam said. And know that when... His statement: (that the increase) means the increase occurring due to the arrival of the surplus quantity over the quantity that is interstitial. His statement: (when the pores occur) due to the abundance of moisture in the specific body and heat. His statement: (in its 'where' or its 'position' or its 'quality') because the 'where'-motion necessitates change of constitution, because the constitutional parts become similar to the nature of the original parts in constitution and color, so they moved in quality as well, so the word 'or' is to prevent vacuum. His statement: (so what the answerer said is correct) because the change of the original parts in magnitude occurred gradually, and the mover in the remaining two states is the same person based on that the nutritive parts are not considered in individuation, otherwise the person would be annihilated moment by moment. So this is not like the joining of water to water and their becoming one continuous entity, preventing increase in the fleshy parts. Then fatness is excluded by the condition of directions, whether an increase in length is also considered in fatness or not, since the increase is not in the directions of the original parts, namely the bones. So reflect. His statement: (so what the answerer said) has consideration because the second whole, which is quantified exactly, has two magnitudes in two times. Or do you not see that a small amount of water, when another water is added to it, they become one thing? Counting growth and withering among the quantitative motions, so the proper view is that fatness and emaciation should also be counted among them. His statement: (so the proper view is that they should be counted, etc.) So the Allamah counted them in his commentary on the Canon, and the wording of the book al-Najat also indicates that. Perhaps the [earlier] scholars omitted mentioning them because their purpose was to state the occurrence of the four divisions of motion obtained by rational division in quantity, not to enumerate all individuals.
Source pages p. 217

Commentary
This passage defines withering (al-dhubul) as the opposite of growth (al-numu), a decrease in size due to separation from all sides naturally. It then distinguishes growth from fatness (al-siman) and emaciation (al-huzal). The key point: growth involves the creation of pores in original parts, entry of additional parts, and expansion in all dimensions proportionally. Al-Razi objects that growth and withering are not quantitative motions because original parts retain their size; he sees only motion in 'where', 'position', or 'quality'. The response defends the quantitative view: original parts increase in size due to integration of additional parts. The author then discusses the condition for the response to hold—the whole must become a single continuous entity. The final glosses comment on specific phrases, and the passage concludes that fatness and emaciation should also be counted as quantitative motions, citing authorities like the Allamah (Ibn Sina?) and the book al-Najat.

Key Terms

الذبول
السمن
الهزال
الأجزاء الأصلية
المنافذ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any increase in body size counts as growth.
Correction: True growth requires proportional expansion in all directions through integration of new parts into original parts; fat accumulation without such integration is not growth.

Checkpoint
What distinguishes true growth from fatness according to al-Razi?
A) Growth occurs only in young people
B) Growth involves expansion of original parts after additional parts enter their pores
C) Fatness is always accompanied by emaciation
D) Growth and fatness are synonymous

Key Takeaway
Growth is a proportional increase in all dimensions through integration of new parts into original parts; fatness is not true growth.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Is your spiritual growth truly penetrating your 'original parts'—your core beliefs, character, and habits—or is it merely adding superficial good deeds like fat on hardened bones? What would it take for nourishment to reach your core?
?
When you look at your life, are you confusing temporary 'fatness'—external blessings, status, or knowledge—with genuine growth that transforms your innermost being? How can you tell the difference?
?
If your 'original parts' have become dry and resistant like the old man's, what must change for you to become soft and receptive again so that true growth can resume? Will you ask Allah tonight to soften your heart?

Logic and Technical Terms

Debating Quantitative Motion: Al-Razi's Objection and the Response
Al-Jurjani presents the debate on whether growth and withering are quantitative motions, with al-Razi's objection and the defense.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Razi's objection to classifying growth and withering as quantitative motions, the response defending it, and the author's conditional resolution.

Core Idea
Al-Razi argued growth is only motion in 'where' or quality; the response says original parts do change magnitude when parts integrate.

After defining growth and withering, al-Jurjani presents a famous debate: Are these truly quantitative motions? Imam al-Razi argues they are not, because original parts remain the same size. A counter-argument insists that integration of new parts does change magnitude. The author then offers his own resolution, hinging on whether the whole becomes a single continuous entity.

Guided Path
1
Read al-Razi's objection
Al-Razi claims that growth and withering are not quantitative motions because original and additional parts each keep their own magnitude. Only motion in 'where', 'position', or 'quality' occurs.
He argues that the parts themselves do not increase in size.
2
Read the response to al-Razi
The response says that when additional parts enter the pores of original parts and become like them, the original parts' magnitude does increase. Denying this is obstinacy.
3
Understand the author's resolution
The author, al-Jurjani, conditionally accepts the response: if the insertion makes the whole a single continuous entity, then quantitative motion occurs. Otherwise, al-Razi's view holds.
He then notes that fatness and emaciation should also be considered quantitative motions, citing other scholars.

Source Text

الورم فليس على نسبة طبيعية. الوجه (الرابع: الذبول ) وهو (عكسه ) أي عكس النمو فهو انتقاص حجم الجسم بسبب ما ينفصل عنه في جميع الأقطار على نسبة طبيعية، قال الإمام الرازي : قد يشتبه النمو والذبول بالسمن والهزال والفرق أنَّ الواقف في النمو قد يسمن كما أن المتزايد في النمو قد يهزل وتحقيقه أن الزيادة إذا أحدثت المنافذ في الأجزاء الأصلية ودخلت فيها وتشبهت بطبيعتها واندفعت الأجزاء الأصلية إلى جميع الأقطار على نسبة واحدة مناسبة لطبيعة النوع فذلك النمو، وأما الشيخ إذا صار سميناً فإنَّ أجزاءه الأصلية قد جفت وصلبت فلا يقوى الغذاء على تفريقها والنفوذ فيها، فلذلك لا تتحرك أعضاؤه الأصلية إلى الزيادة، فلا يكون نامياً لكن لحمه يتحرك إلى الزيادة، فيكون ذلك نمواً في اللحم، إلا أنَّ اسم النمو مخصوص بحركة الأعضاء الأصلية قال : والمشهور أنّ النمو والذبول من الحركات الكمية وهو بعيد عندي فإن الأجزاء الأصلية والزائدة في المغتذى باق كل واحد منها على مقداره الذي كان عليه، نعم ربما تحرك كل واحد منها في أينه أو وضعه أو كيفه، لكن ذلك ليس حركة في الكم وقد أجيب عنه بأنَّ الأجزاء الأصلية زاد مقدارها عند النمو على ما كانت عليه قبل ذلك ضرورة دخول الأجزاء الزائدة في منافذها، وتشبهها بها ونقص مقدارها عند الذبول عما كانت عليه ،قبله، وإنكار هذا مكابرة أقول إن كان اتصال الزائدة بعد المداخلة بالأصلية على وجه يصير به المجموع متصلاً واحداً في نفسه، فالصواب ما قاله المجيب، فالقول ما قاله الإمام واعلم أنه إذا

Motion in Quality: Definition of Transformation
Al-Jurjani defines motion in quality (al-kayf) as transformation (istihalah) with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of transformation (istihalah) and its two necessary conditions.

Core Idea
Transformation is gradual quality-change, not instantaneous.

In this passage, al-Jurjani introduces motion in the category of quality, which he calls transformation (istihalah). He gives concrete examples and then sets two essential conditions. Understanding these conditions is key to distinguishing transformation from other types of change.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and note the example
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Pay attention to the examples of a grape blackening and water heating.
2
Identify the two conditions
The passage explicitly states two requirements: (1) transition from one quality to another, and (2) gradualness. Locate these in the text.
3
Reflect on why gradualness matters
Consider why instantaneous change would not count as transformation. This helps you see the precise definition in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[ الثانية ] من المقولات التي تقع فيها الحركة (الكيف وتسمى الحركة فيه ) بحسب الاصطلاح ( استحالة كما يتسود العنب ويتسخن الماء ) فقد انتقل الجسم من كيفية إلى أخرى على سبيل التدريج فلا بد هاهنا من أمرين أحدهما انتقال الجسم من كيفية إلى أخرى، وثانيهما أنَّ لا يكون ذلك الانتقال دفعة، بل تدريجاً
[The second] of the categories in which motion occurs: quality (al-kayf), and it is called by convention transformation (istihalah), like when a grape blackens and water becomes hot. So the body has transferred from one quality to another gradually. Hence, two things are necessary here: one is the transfer of the body from one quality to another, and the second is that this transfer is not instantaneous but gradual.
Source pages p. 218

Commentary
This passage introduces the second category of motion: motion in quality (al-kayf), termed transformation (istihalah). Examples include a grape turning black (color change) and water heating up. The definition requires two conditions: (1) the body actually changes from one quality to another, and (2) this change occurs gradually over time, not instantaneously. This sets up the standard definition of transformation in kalam.

Key Terms
الكيف
الاستحالة
التدريج
الانتقال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Transformation (istihalah) can occur instantaneously, like a snap change.
Correction: The passage explicitly requires that the change be gradual (tadrij), not instantaneous. Instantaneous change would not fit the definition of motion in kalam.

Checkpoint
What are the two necessary conditions for transformation (istihalah)?
A) Change of place and time
B) Transition from one quality to another, and gradualness
C) Change of substance and accident
D) Increase and decrease

Key Takeaway
Transformation is gradual qualitative change that requires both a transition between qualities and the passage of time.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have desires to change a bad habit or acquire a virtue. Do you expect it to happen overnight? This lesson teaches that genuine transformation is gradual. How does this challenge your impatience? Will you commit to small, consistent steps today?
?
The condition of transformation is a real transfer from one quality to another. Is your repentance merely a formal statement, or has your heart actually moved from heedlessness to awareness? What one quality do you need to shift from, and what quality to, starting now?
?
Consider the grape turning black: it doesn't resist the process. Are you resisting the gradual work of Allah in your soul? What are you holding onto that prevents transformation? Write down one practical step you will take tonight.

Refuting the Theory of Latency (Kumun) in Quality Change
Al-Jurjani presents and refutes the theory of latency and manifestation in quality change.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the theory of latency (kumun) as an alternative explanation for qualitative change and why Sunni kalam rejects it.

Core Idea
Real quality change cannot be explained by latent parts emerging.

This passage presents a theory that denies real qualitative change, claiming instead that all bodies are mixtures of the four natures, with parts being latent or manifest. Al-Jurjani asserts this view is false, though the refutation is partially corrupted in our source. Understanding this theory helps clarify the Islamic position on the reality of change.

Guided Path
1
Read and identify the theory
Read the first part of the passage (up to 'وهذا القول باطل') to understand the latency theory: bodies are mixtures, change is emergence of latent parts.
2
Find the refutation claim
The passage states the theory is false. Read the refutation section, though it is partly garbled, note that Al-Jurjani argues against interpenetration and motion in quantity.
OCR corruption makes the exact argument unclear.
3
Reflect on the implications
Why does kalam insist on real change? This theory denies transformation (istihaalah) and generation/corruption (kawn wa fasaad), which are central to Islamic cosmology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( ومن الناس من أنكر ذلك ) أي انتقال الجسم من كيفية إلى أخرى فالحار عنده لا يصير بارداً ولا البارد حاراً ( وزعم أن ذلك ) الذي يدرك من انقلاب أحدهما إلى الآخر بشهادة الحس ليس تغيراً وانقلاباً في الكيفية، بل هو ( كمون ) واستتار ) لأجزاء كانت متصفة بالصفة الأولى ) كالبرودة مثلا ( وبروز ) وظهور ) لأجزاء كانت متصفة بالصفة الأخرى ) كالحرارة مثلا ( وهما ) أي هذان القسمان من الأجزاء أعني المتصفة بالصفة الأولى والمتصفة بالصفة الأخرى ( موجودان فيه ) أي في ذلك الجسم ( دائماً إلا أن ما يبرز منها أي من تلك الأجزاء ( يحس بها وبكيفيتها ( وما كمن منها ( لا يحس بها ) وبكيفيتها وهؤلاء أعني أصحاب الكمون والبروز زعموا أنَّ الأجسام لا يوجد فيها ما هو بسيط صرف بل كل جسم فإنه مختلط من جميع الطبائع المختلفة، لكنه باسم الغالب الظاهر ، فإذا لقيه ما يكون الغالب عليه من جنس ما كان مغلوباً فيه، فإنه يبرز ذلك المغلوب من الكمون ويحاول مقاومة الغالب حتى يظهر، وتوسلوا بذلك إلى إنكار الاستحالة وإنكار الكون والفساد ( وهذا القول ( باطل وإلا لكانت الأجزاء يسمى عدم الحركة في الكم على ما وهم بخلاف ما إذا لم يصر المجموع متصلاً واحداً في نفسه، بل إذا كان انضمام أجزاء بأجزاء مع ربوط المفاصل.
قوله : (بل) كمون واستثار أشار بالعطف إلى أن معنى الكمون انحصار الأجزاء في باطن الجسم لا بمداخلة فإن تداخل الجوهرين باطل.
قوله : ( أنَّ الأجسام ) أي العنصرية .
قوله : ( من جميع الطبائع المختلفة ) أي الطبائع الأربعة .
أنهما متقدران بالمقدارين في الحالتين، فلم يكن هناك حركة لمقدار وإنما يكون لو كان موضوع واحد بعينه متقدراً بالمقدار الثاني غير المجموع الأول المتقدر بالمقدار الأول بالضرورة، فلم يكن المتقدر متصلاً في نفسه كما عند الحس وليس فيه حركة في المقدار أصلاً.
(And among people are those who denied that) i.e., the transfer of the body from one quality to another. So according to them, what is hot does not become cold, nor does what is cold become hot. (And they claimed that) what is perceived by the sense of one turning into the other is not a change and transformation in quality, but rather it is a latency (kumun) and concealment of parts that were qualified by the first quality, e.g., coldness, and an emergence (buruz) and appearance of parts that were qualified by the other quality, e.g., heat. (And both) i.e., these two divisions of parts, namely those qualified by the first quality and those qualified by the other quality, (are always present in it) i.e., in that body, except that what emerges from them, i.e., from those parts, is perceived with its quality, and what is latent is not perceived with its quality. These, i.e., the proponents of latency and emergence, claimed that bodies have nothing that is purely simple; rather, every body is mixed from all different natures, but it is named by the dominant apparent one. So when it meets what is dominant over it from the same genus as what was overcome in it, then that overcome part emerges from latency and tries to resist the dominant until it appears. And they used this to deny transformation and to deny generation and corruption. (And this statement is false, otherwise the parts would be called... lack of motion in quantity according to what they imagined, unlike when the whole does not become one continuous entity in itself, but only when there is an adjoining of parts to parts with articulation of joints. His statement: (rather) latency and emergence, he indicated by the conjunction that the meaning of latency is the confinement of parts in the interior of the body, not through interpenetration, because the interpenetration of two substances is invalid. His statement: (that the bodies) i.e., elemental bodies. His statement: (from all different natures) i.e., the four natures. That they are quantified by the two magnitudes in the two states, so there is no motion in magnitude. Rather, it would be only if there were one identical subject quantified by the second magnitude other than the first whole necessarily quantified by the first magnitude. So the quantified was not continuous in itself as per sense, and there is no motion in magnitude at all.
Source pages p. 218

Commentary
This passage presents the theory of latency (kumun) and emergence (buruz), which denies real qualitative change. According to this theory, a body always contains parts with both qualities (e.g., hot and cold), but when one set is dominant, the other is latent. Change is merely the emergence of latent parts and concealment of previously manifest parts. The proponents claim bodies are mixtures of all four primary natures. Al-Jurjani refutes this as false. The refutation argument is somewhat fragmented in the OCR, but the point is that this view implies no real motion in quantity or quality, and it relies on an invalid assumption of interpenetration of substances. The glosses clarify that latency means confinement, not interpenetration, and that bodies in question are elemental bodies composed of the four natures. The final gloss suggests that if the whole is not continuous, there is no motion in magnitude.

تداخل الجوهرين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Qualitative change is only apparent; there is no real transformation in bodies.
Correction: Sunni kalam holds that bodies undergo real qualitative change (istihaalah) and generation/corruption (kawn wa fasaad). The latency theory is false.

Checkpoint
What do the proponents of latency (kumun) claim about bodies?
A) Bodies are purely simple
B) Bodies are mixtures of all different natures, with some parts latent and some manifest
C) Bodies have only one quality at a time
D) Bodies change through creation and annihilation

Key Takeaway
The theory of latency denies real change and is rejected by kalam because it implies invalid metaphysical assumptions.

Motion Occurs Only in Quality Among Categories
Author asserts that motion occurs only in the category of quality, not in substance or others.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why motion is confined to the category of quality according to this kalam author, and how substance changes are considered instantaneous generation and corruption rather than motion.

Core Idea
Motion is gradual change only in quality; substance changes are immediate.

This passage from a classical kalam text clarifies the precise meaning of motion within the Aristotelian categories. The author argues that real gradual motion is found only in quality, while changes in other categories, like substance, are instantaneous and thus classified as generation and corruption. This distinction is crucial for understanding the kalam view of physical change.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the original Arabic carefully, then the literal translation. Notice how the author structures the argument in one continuous flow.
The passage begins with 'المرصد الرابع - المقصد الثالث' indicating a new section.
2
Identify the main claim about quality
The author asserts that motion occurs only in the category of quality (كيف). Try to find the sentence that states this and the reasoning that follows about quality being 'non-fixed' (غير قارة).
Look for 'وقوع الحركة من المقولات وتغيراتها غير قارة'.
3
Understand the analogy with time
The author argues that between any two qualities, other qualities can be supposed, just as between any two moments in time there are other moments. This avoids logical contradictions.
This analogy supports the gradual nature of motion in quality.
4
Contrast with substance and other categories
Other categories, especially substance, do not involve gradual motion. The author says substance changes are instantaneous (دفعي), classified as generation and corruption (كون وفساد).
This shows the author's precision: not all change is motion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الرابع - المقصد الثالث : وقع الحركة من المقولات وتغيراتها غير قارة، ففي كل آن يفرض يكون له فيه كيفية أخرى مفروضة ولا يمكن أن يفرض في تلك الكيفية غير القارة كيفيتان متصلتان، بل كل كيفيتين يفرض فيها يمكن أن يفرض فيما بينهما كيفيات أخر، كما أنَّ كل آنين يفرض في الزمان يمكن أن يفرض بينهما آنات أخر فلا يلزم شيء من المحذورات وباقي المقولات لا يقع فيها حركة أما الجوهر فلا شك أنه تتبدل صورته بصورة أخرى لكن هذا التبدل دفعي كما سيأتي فيكون من قبيل الكون والفساد دون الحركة في الجوهر
The Fourth Observation - Third Topic: The occurrence of motion from the categories and its changes are non-fixed. For in every moment that is supposed, it has another supposed quality in it, and it is not possible to suppose in that non-fixed quality two continuous qualities. Rather, every two qualities supposed in it can have other qualities supposed between them, just as every two moments supposed in time can have other moments supposed between them, so none of the impossibilities follow. And the remaining categories do not have motion occurring in them. As for substance, there is no doubt that its form changes to another form, but this change is instantaneous, as will come, so it is of the kind of generation and corruption, not motion in substance.
Source pages p. 222

Commentary
This passage defines the scope of motion within the Aristotelian categories. The author states that motion occurs only in the category of quality (كيف), which is described as non-fixed (غير قارة). The argument is that in every atom of time, a new quality can be supposed, and between any two qualities there can be intermediate qualities, analogous to moments in time. This avoids logical contradictions. Other categories, such as substance, quantity, etc., do not contain motion. For substance (جوهر), form changes instantaneously, which is generation and corruption (كون وفساد), not gradual motion.

جوهر
كون وفساد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All change is motion.
Correction: This passage distinguishes gradual motion (in quality) from instantaneous change (in substance), which is called generation and corruption, not motion.

Checkpoint

B) Quality (كيف)
C) Quantity (كم)
D) All categories

Key Takeaway
Motion in the strict sense occurs only in quality; substance changes are instantaneous and called generation/corruption.

The Role of 'Also' in Ibn Sina's Argument
Commentator explains the two possible meanings of 'also'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the placement of the word 'also' (أيضاً) affects the logical meaning of Ibn Sina's second argument about form and place.

Core Idea
A single word can change the entire logical structure of a philosophical argument.

This passage from the commentary on Ibn Sina's 'al-Isharat' highlights a subtle but crucial point: the location of the word 'also' in the phrase 'and this also' determines which parallel the argument draws. Understanding this clarifies the argument's logic and shows the precision required in philosophical reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation carefully. Notice the phrase 'wa hadha aydan' and how the commentator discusses the placement of 'aydan'.
The Arabic is slightly incomplete at the end, but the key idea is clear.
2
Identify the two possible placements of 'also'
The commentator explains two scenarios: if 'aydan' is placed before 'wa hadha', and if it is placed after. Identify what each placement implies.
Each leads to a different logical parallel.
3
Understand the parallel logic in each case
In the first case, the parallel is between the whole's place and the part's place. In the second, the parallel is between generation-corruption and the attribution of place to form. Grasp how the meaning shifts.
Both interpretations require the form to be caused (haditha).
4
Apply the commentator's summary
The commentator gives an example of air that was previously water. Use this to see how a part's prior form determines its place. This illustrates the first interpretation.
The example shows the practical implication of the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( وهذا أيضاً إلخ) نقل عنه قدس سره لو كان لفظة أيضاً متقدماً على قوله : وهذا لكان معناها ظاهراً ، أي كما أن اختصاص الكل بحيزه لصورته كذلك اختصاص كل جزء بحيزه لصورته، وهذا لا يتصور إذا كانت الصورة حادثة وعلى تقدير تأخيرها كان المعنى كما أن الكون والفساد يستلزم صحة الحدوث، كذلك الاستناد إنما يتصور إذا كانت الصورة حادثة . قوله : ( لكونه متصوراً بصورة أخرى حالها على قياس هذه الصورة) أي لكون الجزء من الجسم متصوراً بصورة سابقة على هذه الصورة مناسبة لذلك الجزء من الحيز، بسبب كالمجاورة والمحاذاة وتلخيصه أن هذا الجزء من الهواء، إنما اختص بهذا الجزء من الحيز لأنه ما كان قبل هذه الصورة متصوراً بصورة مائية، ومتحيزاً في جزء من حيز الماء محاذ لهذا الجزء من
His statement: 'And this also, etc.' It is reported from him – may his secret be hallowed – that if the word 'also' had been placed before his saying 'and this', its meaning would be apparent: just as the assignment of the whole to its place is due to its form, so too the assignment of each part to its place is due to its form. And this is not conceivable if the form is caused. And if [also] is delayed, the meaning would be: just as generation and corruption necessitate the validity of origination, so too the attribution is only conceivable if the form is caused. His statement: 'Because it is formed with another form whose state is analogous to this form' – meaning that the part of the body is formed with a form prior to this form suitable for that part of the place, due to adjacency and alignment. In summary, this part of air is assigned to this part of place because, before this form, it was formed with a watery form, and was situated in a part of the water's place adjacent to this part of
Source pages p. 223

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the phrase 'wa hadha aydan' (and this also) from Ibn Sina's second argument. The commentator explains that the placement of the word 'also' (أيضاً) affects the meaning. If placed before 'wa hadha', it indicates a parallel: just as the whole's place is due to its form, so each part's place is due to its form. This reasoning only works if the form is caused (حادثة). If 'also' is placed later, the meaning shifts to: just as generation and corruption imply origination, so the attribution of place to form requires the form to be caused. The commentator then explains the phrase 'because it is formed with another form' as meaning that the part had a prior appropriate form that situated it in a specific place due to continuity or alignment, using the example of air that was previously water.

اختصاص
صورة
حادثة
مجاورة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The word 'also' is just filler and does not affect the argument's logic.
Correction: In reality, the placement of 'also' changes the entire logical parallel being drawn, showing the precision required in philosophical texts.

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, what does the word 'also' (أيضاً) indicate if placed before 'wa hadha'?
A) That the part's place depends on the whole's place
B) That the part's place depends on its form, just as the whole's place depends on its form
C) That generation and corruption imply origination
D) That the form is eternal

Key Takeaway
The precise placement of 'also' determines whether the argument draws a parallel between whole/part or between generation-corruption and the attribution of place to form.

The commentator insists on precision because he knows that truth hangs on exact wording.
