Logic and Technical Terms
intermediate
8 min
Answering the objection: a single body as both beginning and end
Al-Jurjani resolves an apparent contradiction of a body being both beginning and end.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Jurjani resolves the apparent contradiction of a single body being both the beginning and end of a motion by distinguishing between rectilinear and circular motion and between the body and its endpoints.

Core Idea
A body's being both start and end of motion depends on conceptual consideration, not simultaneous reality.

Al-Jurjani addresses a clever objection: if the concepts of beginning and end are opposites, how can a single body be both the start and end of a motion? The answer hinges on distinguishing between the body itself and the endpoints of motion, and between rectilinear and circular motion. This passage sharpens our understanding of how attributes inhere in subjects.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the first sentence of the source. The objector points out that a single body can serve as both the beginning and end of a motion, questioning how beginning and end can be opposites if they coexist in one subject.
The objection assumes attributes inhere directly in the body.
2
Identify the answer's strategy
Al-Jurjani distinguishes between the body and its endpoints. He states that 'beginning' and 'end' are not primary attributes of the body but of the endpoints (atraf). So the combination is not in the same subject.
This logical move shifts the subject of the attributes.
3
Apply to two types of motion
In rectilinear motion, endpoints are distinct; in circular motion, the same point is considered beginning and end but at different times — never simultaneously. Thus no actual contradiction exists.
This shows the power of conceptual analysis in resolving apparent paradoxes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : قد يكون جسم واحد مبدأ لحركة ومنتهى لها أيضاً فكيف يتصور التضاد بينهما مع اجتماعهما في موضوع واحد قلت : هما أعني مفهومي المبدأ والمنتهى غير عارضين للجسم عروضاً أولياً يقال إنهما يجتمعان فيه بل هما عارضان للأطراف الحاصلة في الأجسام ولا يكون طرف واحد مبدأ ومنتهى لحركة واحدة إلا بالفرض وفي زمانين إذ لا يتصور في حركة واحدة مستقيمة أن يكون مبدؤها ومنتهاها طرفاً واحداً، وأما المستديرة فإن مبدأها ومنتهاها نقطة واحدة مفروضة لكنها لا تتصف بهاتين الصفتين في آن واحد فهي وإن كانت واحدة بالذات إلا أنها اثنتان في الاعتبار، وذلك كاف لها في كونها بداية للحركة ونهاية لها.
For it is said: A single body can be the beginning (mabda') of a motion and also its end (muntaha); so how can contrariety (tadhad) be imagined between them while they are combined in one subject? I say: They – meaning the concepts of beginning and end – are not primary attributes (aawarid) of the body such that they are said to combine in it; rather they are attributes of the endpoints (atraf) existing in bodies. And one endpoint is not a beginning and an end of a single motion except by assumption and in two times. For it is not conceivable in a single rectilinear motion that its beginning and its end be one endpoint. As for circular motion, its beginning and its end are one assumed point, but it is not characterized by these two attributes at one moment; so even though it is one in essence, it is two in consideration, and that suffices for it to be a beginning and an end of the motion.
Source pages p. 253

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer. The objector asks: how can the concepts of beginning and end be opposite if a single body can be both the start and the end of a motion? The answer clarifies that these concepts do not primarily inhere in the body itself but in the endpoints (atraf) of the motion. In rectilinear motion, the endpoints are distinct points; the same point cannot be both. In circular motion, the same point can be considered both beginning and end, but not at the same time – it is one point in essence but two in conceptual consideration, which is sufficient for it to serve as both.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The continuity of circular motion implies that the same point is simultaneously beginning and end.
Correction: Al-Jurjani explains that the same point is considered beginning and end only in different times or respects, not simultaneously.

Checkpoint
According to al-Jurjani, why can a single body not be said to be both the beginning and end of a rectilinear motion?
A) Because the concepts of beginning and end are opposites that cannot combine in any subject
B) Because the body is not the primary subject of these attributes; rather, the endpoints are, and one endpoint cannot be both in a single rectilinear motion
C) Because rectilinear motion has no beginning or end
D) Because the body can only be the beginning, not the end

Key Takeaway
The apparent contradiction of a body being both beginning and end of motion is resolved by recognizing that the attributes 'beginning' and 'end' inhere in endpoints, never simultaneously in the same point within a single motion.

Rectilinear Motion Is Not Opposite to Circular Motion
Al-Jurjani proves rectilinear motion is not opposite to circular motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the proof that rectilinear motion is not opposite to circular motion, based on the principle that one thing has only one opposite.

Core Idea
One thing cannot have multiple opposites; infinite arcs prove rectilinear motion has no single opposite.

Al-Jurjani presents a detailed argument to show that rectilinear motion and circular motion are not opposites. He uses geometric reasoning and the logical principle that a single thing can have only one opposite. This lesson walks you through the steps of his proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without overanalyzing, then read the literal translation. Let the flow of the argument sink in.
Pay attention to the geometric terms: chord (وتر) and arc (قوس).
2
Identify the key principle
The argument relies on the principle 'ضد الواحد واحد' (the opposite of one thing is one). Locate where this appears and understand its role.
3
Trace the infinite regress argument
Follow the reasoning: if rectilinear motion were opposite to circular motion, then for any straight line (chord) there would be infinitely many arcs that are more curved, leading to an infinite number of opposites. This contradicts the principle.
Consider why each successive arc would be 'more worthy' of opposition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحركة المستقيمة لا تضاد الحركة المستديرة وإلا كان ذلك بسبب تضاد أطراف المستقيمة والمستديرة وهو باطل إذ كل مستقيمة فإنها واقعة على خط هو وتر لقسي غير متناهية بالقوة فلو كانت المستقيمة ضداً للمستديرة لكان للمستقيمة الواحدة بالشخص أضداد غير متناهية متخالفة بالنوع هي المستديرات المتوهمة من المستقيمة إلى مبدئها، وذلك باطل إذ ضد الواحد واحد كما مر في مباحث التضاد، وأيضاً كل قوس يفرض ضداً لذلك الخط فهناك قوس أخرى أعظم تحدباً من الأولى، فتكون هذه بالضدية أولى فليس شيء من تلك القسي ضاداً للمستقيم، فلا يكون المستقيم ضداً لشيء منها
Rectilinear motion is not opposite to circular motion; otherwise that would be due to the opposition of the endpoints of the rectilinear and the circular, and it is false. For every rectilinear motion lies on a line that is the chord of potentially infinite arcs. So if rectilinear motion were opposite to circular motion, then one particular rectilinear motion would have infinite opposites differing in species – namely the arcs imagined from the rectilinear to its beginning. And that is false, for the opposite of one thing is one thing, as has passed in the investigations of contrariety. Also, for every arc assumed opposite to that line, there is another arc more curved than the first; so this one would be more worthy of opposition. Thus nothing among those arcs is opposite to the rectilinear, so the rectilinear is not opposite to any of them.
Source pages p. 254

Commentary
This passage presents a proof that rectilinear motion is not opposite to circular motion. The author argues that if they were opposite, it would be because of opposition between their endpoints. But that is false because any straight line (the path of rectilinear motion) can be the chord of infinitely many arcs (paths of circular motions). If rectilinear motion were opposite to each of those circular motions, then one rectilinear motion would have infinitely many opposites, which contradicts the principle that one thing has only one opposite. Additionally, for any assumed opposite arc, one can find a more curved arc that would be more strongly opposite, leading to an infinite regress. Therefore, no circular motion is opposite to a given rectilinear motion, so rectilinear motion is not opposite to circular motion.

Key Terms
حركة مستقيمة
حركة مستديرة
تضاد
وتر
قوس
ضد الواحد واحد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Rectilinear and circular motions are opposites because they are different in kind.
Correction: Opposition requires specific conditions; the proof shows that infinite arcs would be needed, violating the principle that one thing has one opposite.

Checkpoint
What principle is used to refute the possibility that rectilinear motion is opposite to circular motion?
A) The opposite of one thing is one thing (ضد الواحد واحد)
B) Circular motion has no beginning or end
C) Rectilinear motion is faster than circular motion
D) Opposites cannot exist in the same subject

Key Takeaway
Rectilinear motion and circular motion are not opposites because a single rectilinear motion would have infinitely many circular opposites, violating the principle that one thing has only one opposite.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed two ideas or people were opposites simply because they seemed different, without truly examining the basis of opposition? This lesson shows that opposition requires a precise condition. Choose a conflict in your life and ask: Is it true opposition, or mere difference? How can you identify the real opposite?
?
If one thing can have only one opposite, what does that mean for your heart's devotion? Could loving something fully leave room for its opposite? Consider what truly opposes your love for Allah in your life right now. Are you giving that opposite space?
?
The proof uses infinite arcs to reveal impossibility. We often limit our reasoning to obvious possibilities. What difficult truth about your own soul might you be avoiding because you haven't considered the full spectrum of consequences? How can you train yourself to think beyond the finite, as the proof does?

Refutation of Opposition in Circular Motions
Refutes the claim that circular motions are mutually opposite.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why circular motions are not truly opposite despite apparent differences in direction, and what conditions constitute real opposition in kalam.

Core Idea
Conceptual difference is not real opposition; true opposition requires inherent difference.

This lesson addresses a key debate in kalam about whether circular motions can be considered opposite. An opponent argued that since the arcs of a circle are equal, no real opposition exists. The author refutes this by distinguishing conceptual from real opposition, and referencing the classical work al-Mulakhkhas.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the translation. Let the author's argument flow naturally.
Notice the technical terms like تضاد and مغايرة اعتبارية.
2
Identify the opponent's claim
The opponent states that because the two halves of a circular path are equal in essence and endpoints, circular motions cannot be opposite.
This is the starting point of the refutation.
3
Understand the author's initial response
The author argues that if you consider each half simultaneously, the motions differ in beginning and end, thus they are opposite. He gives the Cancer-Capricorn example.
The key is the difference in the two beginnings and endings.
4
Grasp the complete revolution perspective
When considering the full revolution, the start and end coincide, making the difference merely directional. This leads to the discussion from al-Mulakhkhas.
This shows that the opposition can be resolved by a different perspective.
5
Reflect on the requirement for real opposition
The author concludes that real opposition requires essential or necessary accident opposition, as in ascending and descending. Conceptual difference alone does not suffice.
This is the key takeaway: not all differences are real oppositions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الرابع - المقصد الثامن : الحركات المتضادة بين الصاعدة والهابطة النصفان متساويين في الماهية وكذلك الأطراف والنهايات متساوية فيها، فلا يكون شيء منها سبباً لتضاد الحركات المستديرة، فلا تكون متضادة، قال المصنف : ( ولا يخفى ما فيه من أن الحركة في النصفين مع اتحاد المسافة مختلفة ) يعني أن ما ذكروه إنما يدل على أن الحركة إلى التوالي والحركة إلى خلافه إذا اعتبر حالهما في نصفين متبادلين كانتا متماثلين متحدتين في المبدأ والمنتهى، فلا يتصور بهذا الاعتبار بينهما تضاد ولا شك أنه إذا اعتبر حالهما في كل واحد من النصفين معاً كانتا متخالفتين بل متضادتين، فإن حركة المنحدر من السرطان إلى الجدي على التوالي مضادة لحركة الصاعد من الجدي إلى السرطان على خلاف التوالي للتضاد بين المبدأين والمنتهيين، وإن كانا مفروضين مع اتحاد المسافة على قياس الصاعدة والهابطة المستقيمتين، وكذا الحال في الصاعدة من الجدي إلى السرطان على التوالي والمنحدرة من السرطان إلى الجدي على خلاف التوالي نعم إذا اعتبر تمام الدورة فيهما اتحدت المسافة وكانت نقطة واحدة مبدأ ومنتهى لهما معاً وكان الاختلاف بينهما بحسب التوجه منها وإليها وذكر في الملخص أن أمثال هذه المباحث لفظية لأنه إن أريد بالضدين كل معنيين وجوديين يمتنع اجتماعهما دفعة واحدة في محل واحد كانت الحركة المستقيمة مضادة للمستديرة وكانت المستديرات أيضاً متضادة لامتناع الاجتماع، وإن أريد مع ذلك أن يكون ما منه وما إليه أموراً موجودة فلا تضاد حينئذ بين المستقيمة والمستديرة ولا بين المستديرات . قوله : ( ولا يخفى إلخ) مقصود ذلك القائل أن الحركتين المذكورتين ليس بينهما غاية الخلاف، إذ يفعل كل منهما بفعل الآخر وتحد غايتها، وإنما عرض الخلاف باعتبار فرض مبدأ كلتيهما ومنتهاه مخالفاً للأخرى وذلك مغايرة اعتبارية لا يوجب تضاد الحركتين كما مر منقولاً من الشفاء. قوله : ( وإن كانا مفروضين ) قد عرفت أن تغاير المبدأ والمنتهى بالفرض لا يصحح التضاد بينهما ولا بد في تضاد الحركات من تضادهما بالذات أو باعتبار عارض لازم كما في الصاعدة والهابطة. قوله : وذلك في الملخص إلخ فيه بحث لأن الكلام في التضاد الحقيقي المعتبر في غاية الخلاف وأنه إنما يكون في الحركات بسبب تضاد المبدأ والمنتهى والنزاع في أنه هل يتحقق ذلك بين الحركة المستقيمة وبين المستديرات أو لا ؟ فالنزاع معنوي . قوله : ( وكذلك الأطراف والنهايات متساوية فيها ) فإن قلت الأطراف متحدة في المثال المذكور لا متساوية قلت : الحكم بالتساوي مبني على المغايرة الاعتبارية . قوله : ( للتضاد بين المبدأين والمنتهيين ) أي باعتبار المبدئية والمنتهية وإن كان ذاتاهما متحدين بالماهية.
The Fourth Observation - The Eighth Topic: Opposing motions between ascending and descending: the two halves are equal in essence, and likewise the extremities and endpoints are equal in it. So none of them is a cause for the opposition of circular motions, so they are not opposite. The author said: (And it is not hidden what is in it: that motion in the two halves, with the unity of distance, is different). Meaning that what they mentioned only indicates that motion in one direction and motion in the opposite direction, if their state is considered in two alternating halves, are similar, united in beginning and end. So opposition between them is not conceivable in this consideration. And there is no doubt that if their state is considered in each of the two halves together, they are different, even opposite. For the motion of descent from Cancer to Capricorn in one direction is opposite to the motion of ascent from Capricorn to Cancer in the opposite direction due to the opposition between the two beginnings and the two endings, even if they are assumed with unity of distance on the analogy of rectilinear ascending and descending motions. And likewise the case in the ascent from Capricorn to Cancer in one direction and the descent from Cancer to Capricorn in the opposite direction. Yes, if the complete revolution is considered in both, the distance becomes one, and a single point becomes beginning and end for both together, and the difference between them is by way of direction from it and to it. And it is mentioned in al-Mulakhkhas that such investigations are verbal, because if by 'two opposites' is meant every two existential meanings whose simultaneous occurrence in a single subject is impossible, then rectilinear motion is opposite to circular motion, and circular motions are also opposite due to the impossibility of coexistence. And if it is meant additionally that the 'from which' and 'to which' are existent things, then there is no opposition then between rectilinear and circular, nor between circular motions. His statement: (And it is not hidden, etc.) The intention of that speaker is that the two mentioned motions do not have the utmost difference, since each is done by the act of the other and determines its limit. Rather, difference occurred by way of assuming the beginning and end of each to be opposite to the other, and that is a conceptual difference that does not necessitate opposition of the two motions, as has been transmitted from al-Shifa. His statement: (And even if they are assumed) You have known that the difference of beginning and end by assumption does not validate opposition between them, and for opposition of motions there must be opposition in essence or by virtue of a necessary accident, as in ascending and descending. His statement: (And that in al-Mulakhkhas, etc.) There is discussion in it, because the talk is about real opposition considered in the utmost difference, and that it occurs in motions only due to opposition of beginning and end, and the dispute is whether that is realized between rectilinear motion and circular motions or not? So the dispute is conceptual. His statement: (And likewise the extremities and endpoints are equal in it) For if you say: the extremities are identical in the mentioned example, not equal, I say: the judgment of equality is based on conceptual difference. His statement: (Due to opposition between the two beginnings and the two endings) i.e., by virtue of beginning-ness and end-ness, even if their essences are united in essence.
Source pages p. 256

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an earlier argument. It begins with the Eighth Topic, which addresses whether circular motions are opposite to each other. The author explains that an opponent argued that since the two halves of a circular path are equal in essence, and the endpoints are also equal, there is no basis for opposition among circular motions. The author then responds by clarifying that if we consider two halves of the circle alternately, the motions seem similar, but if we consider each half separately, they are actually opposite because the beginnings and ends differ. He gives the example of motion from Cancer to Capricorn and from Capricorn to Cancer: in one direction it is descending, in the other ascending, and thus they are opposite just like rectilinear ascending and descending motions. However, if the complete revolution is considered, the circumference is one and the start and end coincide, so the difference is only directional. The author then references al-Mulakhkhas, which suggests the dispute is merely verbal: if 'opposite' means two existents that cannot coexist simultaneously in the same subject, then circular motions are opposite (since they cannot coexist). But if it additionally requires that the 'from which' and 'to which' are actual existents (not merely endpoints of a completed circle), then there is no opposition between rectilinear and circular, nor among circular motions. The subsequent marginal comments (القول) elaborate: the claim that the two motions do not have utmost difference, the need for intrinsic opposition, the conceptual nature of the dispute, and the distinction between identity and equality.

Key Terms

حركات مستديرة
مبدأ ومنتهى
مغايرة اعتبارية
الصاعدة والهابطة
المباحث اللفظية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Conceptual difference (مغايرة اعتبارية) suffices for real opposition between motions.
Correction: The author clarifies that real opposition requires intrinsic difference (either in essence or by necessary accident), not mere conceptual difference.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is required for two motions to be truly opposite?
A) Difference in distance covered
B) Opposition in the two beginnings and endings either essentially or by a necessary accident
C) Being in different directions
D) Being rectilinear rather than circular

Key Takeaway
Circular motions are not truly opposite unless their beginnings and ends are essentially or necessarily opposite; conceptual difference alone is insufficient.

Commit to one specific step you will take to resolve any such conceptual confusion.
?

In your own spiritual life, do you sometimes mistake your limited understanding for genuine contradiction? Motion as Accidental Quantity and Its Three Divisions
Sanusi defines motion as accidental quantity and lists three modes of its division.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why motion is considered an accidental quantity and the three ways it can be divided.

Core Idea
Motion is not a self‑standing quantity but divisible by distance, time, and the moving body.

This passage from Sharh al-Mawaqif clarifies the ontological status of motion: it is not a primary quantity but accidental. Yet, it exhibits division in three distinct ways—by distance, time, and the moving body—each with its own implications. Al-Sanusi’s exposition helps the student appreciate how motion relates to space, time, and substance.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic text once, noting the initial claim that motion is not a quantity in essence but accidental, followed by the three enumerated divisions.
The parentheses are part of the original text structure.
2
Focus on the first division: by distance
Understand why motion is divisible according to distance: because local motion corresponds to distance, and distance is divisible (no indivisible part).
This applies only to local motion.
3
Then the second division: by time
Notice that time is an accident of motion, so motion divides as time divides. This division is distinct from division by distance, as seen in fast vs. slow motions.
These two divisions can differ.
4
Finally the third division: by the moving body
The body is divisible, so when it moves, each part has its own motion. If the body actually separates, each part has actual motion. Motion follows its subject.
This is analogous to blackness in a body.
5
Reflect on the overall significance
Ask yourself why these distinctions matter: they show that motion, though not a primary quantity, is still quantifiable in multiple ways, which is crucial for kalam discussions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقصد التاسع : الحركة ليست كماً بل عرضية ( الحركة ليست كماً بالذات فإنها من المقولات النسبية لا من مقولة الكم (بل) هي كم ( بالعرض ويعرض لها بسبب الكمية العرضية ( ثلاثة أنواع من الانقسام : الأول بحسب المسافة لانطباقهما ) فإن الحركة الأينية منطبقة على المسافة كأنها حالة فيها والمسافة منقسمة لانتفاء الجزء الذي لا يتجزأ فتنقسم الحركة بانقسامها ( فالحركة إلى نصفها نصف الحركة إلى كلها الثاني بحسب الزمان لأنه عارض لها فينقسم بانقسام عارضها ( فالحركة في نصف ساعة نصف الحركة في ساعة وهذا الانقسام الثابت للحركة بحسب الزمان ( غير ) الانقسام ( الذي بحسب المسافة إذ قد يختلفان كالسريعة والبطيئة ) فإنه إذا فرض اتحادهما في المسافة والانقسام بحسبها، فلا بد أن يختلف زمانهما والانقسام ،بحسبه، وإذا فرض اتحادهما في الزمان والانقسام بحسبه كانتا مختلفتين في الساعة والانقسام بحسبها ( الثالث : بحسب المتحرك فإن الجسم هو المتحرك وهو قابل للقسمة ولا شبهة في أنه ( إذا تحرك ) الجسم ( تحركت أجزاؤه المفروضة فيه والحركة القائمة بكل جزء غير القائمة بالآخر) فقد انقسمت الحركة أيضاً انقساماً فرضياً كمحلها ( فإذا عرض له) أي للجسم (انفصال خارجي ( حصل لكل جزء حركة بالفعل) فالحركة تابعة لمحلها في الانقسام الفرضي والفعلي الخارجي كالسواد القائم بالجسم فإنه يتبعه في هذين الانقسامين، وقد نبهناك على أن الانقسام بحسب المسافة إنما يتصور في الحركة الأينية.
The Ninth Objective: Motion is not a quantity but accidental. Motion is not a quantity in essence, for it is of the relative categories, not of the category of quantity; rather, it is a quantity by accident and occurs to it due to accidental quantity three types of division: the first is according to distance because of their correspondence. For local motion corresponds to distance as if it is a state in it, and distance is divisible due to the rejection of the indivisible part, so motion divides by its division. Thus, motion to half of it is half of motion to all of it. The second is according to time, because it is an accident to it, so it divides by the division of its accident. Thus, motion in half an hour is half of motion in an hour. This division of motion according to time is different from the division according to distance, for they may differ, as in fast and slow. For if they are assumed to be unified in distance and division according to it, then their time and division according to it must differ; and if they are assumed to be unified in time and division according to it, they are different in speed and division according to it. The third: according to the moving body, for the body is the moving entity and is divisible, and there is no doubt that when the body moves, its supposed parts move, and the motion subsisting in each part is other than the motion subsisting in the other. So motion also divides by a supposed division like its subject. Then if external separation occurs to it (i.e., the body), each part obtains actual motion. Thus, motion follows its subject in supposed and actual external division, like blackness subsisting in the body, for it follows it in these two divisions. And we have alerted you that the division according to distance is only conceived in local motion.
Source pages p. 257

Commentary
This passage defines the nature of motion and its divisions. It begins by stating that motion is not a quantity in itself; rather, it belongs to the relative categories (al-maqūlāt al-nisbiyya). However, it is considered a quantity by accident due to an accidental quantity that attaches to it. The passage then enumerates three types of division that apply to motion. First, division according to distance: local motion corresponds to the distance traversed, and since distance is divisible (no indivisible part), the motion itself becomes divisible accordingly. Second, division according to time: because time is an accident of motion, motion divides as time divides. These two divisions are distinct; for example, when a fast and a slow motion cover the same distance, their times differ. Third, division according to the moving body: the body is divisible, and when it moves, each part has its own motion, so the whole motion is divided correspondingly. The passage also clarifies that if the body actually separates, each part has its own actual motion. Finally, it notes that division according to distance applies specifically to local motion.

Key Terms
الحركة
كم بالعرض
الانقسام
المسافة
الزمان
المتحرك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Motion itself is a primary quantity.
Correction: The text clarifies motion is only accidentally a quantity; it is fundamentally a relative category.

Checkpoint
How many types of division does the author mention for motion?
A) Two
B) Three
C) Four
D) One

Key Takeaway
Motion is not a quantity in itself but accidentally, and it divides according to distance, time, and its subject.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider the movements of your daily life—walking, talking, thinking—do you ever reflect that these motions are not self-standing but depend on distance, time, and your body? How does this shift your awareness of the transient and dependent nature of your actions?
?
The lesson distinguishes between division by distance and division by time, noting that they can differ (fast vs. slow). Think of a moment when you felt time 'flew' or 'dragged' during the same activity. What does this teach you about the duality of your experience and the objective reality of motion as the text describes it?
?
If motion follows its subject in division, and your body is composite and changing, what does that imply about the stability of your identity as you move through life? How might this truth call you to anchor your sense of self in something beyond physical motion?

Speed and Slowness Are Not Specific Differences
A note clarifying that speed and slowness differences do not indicate different types of motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why differences in speed and slowness are not considered specific differences (فصول) that distinguish types of motion.

Core Idea
Relative speed does not change the essence of motion.

This short note from Sharh al-Mawaqif addresses a subtle point in logic and natural philosophy: if motions can be fast or slow, does that make them different in kind? The author argues no, because speed and slowness are relative and gradable—properties that specific differences (فصول) cannot have. Let's examine the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice it begins with 'تنبيه' indicating an important clarification.
The passage is concise; read carefully.
2
Identify the two main arguments
The passage gives two reasons why speed/slowness are not specific differences: (1) relativity: the same motion is fast relative to one and slow relative to another, while its essence remains one; (2) gradability: speed and slowness can be intensified or diminished, but differentiae do not admit degrees.
3
Understand the logical principle
The key logical principle is that specific differences (فصول) are not subject to intensification and diminution. If an attribute can vary in degree, it cannot be a differentia.
This principle comes from Aristotelian logic.
4
Check your understanding
Answer the checkpoint question to confirm you grasp why speed and slowness are not differentiae.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تنبيه : الاختلاف بالسرعة والبطء ليس اختلافاً بالنوع فإن الحركة الواحدة سريعة بالنسبة إلى حركة وبطيئة بالنسبة إلى أخرى مع أن ماهيتهما واحدة لا اختلاف فيها ولأنهما أي السرعة والبطء قابلان للاشتداد والتنقص فإن المسافة الواحدة يمكن قطعها بحركات مختلفة في مراتب السرعة والبطء، فلا يكونان فصلين للحركات لأن الفصول لا تقبل الاشتداد والتنقص
Note: The difference in speed and slowness is not a difference in type, for one motion is fast relative to a motion and slow relative to another, while their essence is one with no difference in it, and because both of them, i.e. speed and slowness, are susceptible to intensification and diminution. For one distance can be traversed by different motions in degrees of speed and slowness, so they cannot be differentiae of motions, because differentiae do not admit intensification and diminution.
Source pages p. 263

Commentary
This passage is a note (تنبيه) that addresses a potential misunderstanding: that varying speeds and slownesses indicate that motions are different in kind (نوع). The text argues that the same motion can be fast compared to one motion and slow compared to another, while the essence (ماهية) of the motion remains one and the same. Moreover, speed and slowness can both be intensified and diminished (قابلان للاشتداد والتنقص): the same distance can be covered by motions with different degrees of speed and slowness. Since specific differences (فصول) are not subject to intensification and diminution, speed and slowness cannot serve as differentiae that divide motions into distinct species.

Key Terms
السرعة
البطء
النوع
الفصل
الاشتداد
التنقص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that a fast motion and a slow motion must be different types of motion because they differ in speed.
Correction: The passage explains that speed and slowness are relative and admit degrees, so they do not constitute specific differences. The same motion can be both fast and slow depending on comparison, and speed can vary along a continuum without changing the motion's essence.

Checkpoint
Why does the passage argue that speed and slowness are not specific differences (فصول) of motion?
A) Because they are relative and admit intensification and diminution
B) Because they are essential to motion
C) Because they are not real properties
D) Because they are only found in natural motions

Key Takeaway
Speed and slowness, being relative and gradable, cannot serve as the specific difference that distinguishes one type of motion from another.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your spiritual journey, do you often compare your pace of worship or devotion to others, feeling either proud or discouraged? How can this lesson—that speed and slowness are accidental and do not affect the essence of actions—help you refocus on the sincerity and quality of your deeds rather than their relative speed?
?
When you notice someone who appears to be progressing slowly in their religious practice, do you unconsciously assume they are defective in their faith? This lesson teaches that slowness is gradable and relative; what specific step can you take this week to withhold judgment and instead support them in a way that honors the essence of their faith?

Universality of Rest and the Finitude of Dimensions
Clarifies universality of rest via finitude of dimensions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the claim about rest between motions is made universal by combining it with the finitude of dimensions, leading to the conclusion that motions are not instantaneous.

Core Idea
Rest between motions is universal only when combined with the finitude of dimensions.

Sanusi's first marginal note clarifies that the universality of the claim about rest depends on incorporating the doctrine of the finitude of dimensions. Without that, the argument does not lead to the conclusion that dimensions and time-preserving motion are not instantaneous.

Guided Path
1
Read the note and translation
Read the Arabic passage once and then the literal translation. Notice the key terms: universality (كلية) and finitude of dimensions (تناهي الأبعاد).
2
Identify the logical connection
The note states that universality is obtained by including the finitude of dimensions. Understand that the argument for rest between motions only works if dimensions are finite.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
The ultimate conclusion is that dimensions and the motion that preserves time are not instantaneous. Ask yourself: why does finitude of dimensions lead to non-instantaneous motion?
This connects to broader kalam arguments about time and motion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( ومحصول ما ذكروه (إلخ لا يخفى أن هذه الكلية يحصل من ثبوت هذه المسألة مع ضم مسألة تناهي الأبعاد من التأويل بأن يقال: إن المقصود مما ذكروه ثبوت هذه الكلية بضم تناهي الأبعاد ليتوصل بتلك الكلية إلى أن الأبعاد والحركة الحافظة للزمان ليست آنية .
قوله : ( والأظهر أن يقال إلخ) لأن السابق يرد عليه أن الوصول إذا فرض زمانياً يكون حاصلاً في مجموع ،النصفين لا في كل واحد منهما فالترديد لا معنى له فلا بد من التعرض بعد انقسام ، الحد الذي إليه الوصول حتى لا يكون الوصول في مجموع النصفين وبعد التعرض لذلك لا حاجة إلى الترديد المذكور .
قوله : ( إلى أن بين كل حركتين مستقيمتين سكوناً) قد أشير في أوائل المقصد الثالث من هذا الفصل إلى أن هذا الحكم عندهم لا يختص بالحركات الأينية كما يشعر وصف الحركة هاهنا بالمستقيمة بل يعم غيرها وبهذا أبطل المصنف وقوع الحركة في مقولة أن ينفعل كما مر هناك .
قوله : ( وأكثر المتكلمين من المعتزلة) سياق كلامه يدل على أن أهل السنة أيضاً من المانعين، وكان منعهم لعدم تمام دليل الإثبات عندهم ، لا لأن لهم دليلاً على النفي بخلاف المعتزلة فإن لهم دليلاً على ذلك كما سيأتي ولهذا قيد هاهنا أكثر المتكلمين بكونه من المعتزلة .
His statement: '(And the gist of what they mentioned...) It is not hidden that this universality is obtained from the establishment of this issue together with the issue of the finitude of dimensions, by interpretation that what is intended by what they mentioned is the establishment of this universality by including the finitude of dimensions, in order to arrive by that universality to the fact that dimensions and the motion that preserves time are not instantaneous.
His statement: '(And the more apparent is to say...)' because the previous argument is refuted by the fact that if arrival is assumed to be temporal, it occurs in the sum of the two halves, not in each one of them, so the disjunction is meaningless. So one must mention after the division of the limit to which arrival occurs so that arrival is not in the sum of the two halves, and after mentioning that, there is no need for the aforementioned disjunction.
His statement: '(So that between every two straight-line motions there is a rest)' – it was indicated in the beginning of the third purpose of this chapter that this ruling according to them is not specific to locative motions, as the description of motion here as straight suggests, but rather applies to others, and by this the author invalidated the occurrence of motion in the category of being affected, as passed there.
His statement: '(And most of the theologians from the Mu'tazila)' – the context of his speech indicates that the Ahl al-Sunna are also among the deniers, and their denial was due to the incompleteness of the proof of affirmation according to them, not because they have a proof for negation, unlike the Mu'tazila, for they have a proof for that as will come, and for this reason he qualified here 'most of the theologians' as being from the Mu'tazila.
Source pages p. 265

Commentary
This passage consists of four marginal notes that clarify points in the thirteenth purpose. The first note addresses the universality of the claim about rest, explaining that it is established by combining the issue of rest between motions with the finitude of dimensions, leading to the conclusion that dimensions and time-preserving motion are not instantaneous. The second note refines the proof about arrival: if arrival were temporal, it would occur over the sum of halves, making the original disjunction invalid; thus one must consider the division of the limit. The third note explains that the ruling about rest between straight motions is not limited to locative motions but applies to other categories as well, and that the author used this to refute motion in the category of being affected. The fourth note clarifies that Ahl al-Sunna also deny the necessity of rest, but their denial stems from lack of a complete proof for affirmation, unlike the Mu'tazila who have a positive proof.

Key Terms
كلية
تناهي الأبعاد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The existence of straight-line motions
B) The finitude of dimensions
C) The instantaneous nature of time
D) The denial of motion in place

Key Takeaway
The universality of rest between motions is not independent but depends on the finite nature of dimensions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Refining the Argument about Arrival
Refines the argument about arrival by criticizing the disjunction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the original disjunction about arrival is invalid if arrival is temporal, and how the correct argument must consider the division of the limit.

Core Idea
Temporal arrival occurs in the sum of halves, making the original disjunction meaningless.

The second note addresses a flaw in a previous argument about arrival (al-wusul). If arrival is assumed to be temporal, it would occur over the sum of both halves, not in each half individually, so the either-or disjunction fails. The fix is to explicitly consider the division of the limit.

Guided Path
1
Read the note and translation
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Note the term 'الوصول' (arrival) and 'الترديد' (disjunction).
2
Understand the problem
The original argument presented a disjunction: either arrival is instantaneous or temporal. The note argues that if arrival is temporal, it occurs in the sum of both halves, not in each half individually, so the disjunction is meaningless.
3
Understand the solution
The solution is to mention the division of the limit (الحد) to which arrival occurs. By dividing the limit, one can avoid arrival being in the sum of halves, and then the disjunction is no longer needed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Generalization of Rest to Non-Locative Motions
Explains that the rest ruling applies to all motion categories.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the ruling about rest between straight motions applies to all categories of motion, not just locative, and that this refutes motion in the category of being affected.

Core Idea
The rule about rest is not limited to locative motions; it extends to other categories.

The third marginal note clarifies an important point: when the author speaks of 'straight motions,' it might seem to refer only to motions in place (locative motions). However, the context shows that the ruling applies generally to all types of motion. The author used this generality to refute the occurrence of motion in the category of being affected (an yanfa'il).

Guided Path
1
Read the note and translation
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the terms 'حركات أينية' (locative motions) and 'مقولة أن ينفعل' (category of being affected).
2
Identify the generalization
The note states that the ruling about rest between straight motions is not specific to locative motions, as the description 'straight' might suggest, but rather applies to other categories as well.
3
Understand the consequence
The author used this general ruling to invalidate the idea of motion in the category of being affected. Reflect on why the existence of rest between any two straight motions would preclude motion in that category.

What did the author invalidate using the general ruling about rest between straight motions?
A) The existence of locative motion
B) Motion in the category of being affected
C) The existence of straight-line motion
D) The finitude of dimensions

Key Takeaway
The rest ruling is universal across motion categories, enabling refutation of motion in the category of being affected.

Clarifies why only Mu'tazila are mentioned as deniers of rest.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the difference between Ahl al-Sunna and Mu'tazila regarding their denial of rest between motions: Ahl al-Sunna lack a proof for affirmation, while Mu'tazila have a proof for negation.

Core Idea
Ahl al-Sunna deny rest due to insufficient proof, not a positive counter-proof.

The final marginal note addresses a sectarian nuance. Both Ahl al-Sunna and Mu'tazila are deniers of the necessity of rest, but for different reasons. The context indicates that Ahl al-Sunna do not have a proof for negation, only skepticism, whereas Mu'tazila have a positive argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the note and translation
Read the Arabic and translation. Identify the two groups: Ahl al-Sunna and Mu'tazila.
2
Understand the differing rationales
The note says Ahl al-Sunna deny because they find the proof for affirmation incomplete. The Mu'tazila, on the other hand, have their own proof for the negation of rest.

The author says 'most theologians from the Mu'tazila', which implies that Ahl al-Sunna are also among the deniers, but they are not included in the phrase because their denial is not based on a positive proof.

His statement: '(And the gist of what they mentioned...) It is not hidden that this universality is obtained from the establishment of this issue together with the issue of the finitude of dimensions, by interpretation that what is intended by what they mentioned is the establishment of this universality by including the finitude of dimensions, in order to arrive by that universality to the fact that dimensions and the motion that preserves time are not instantaneous.
His statement: '(And the more apparent is to say...)' because the previous argument is refuted by the fact that if arrival is assumed to be temporal, it occurs in the sum of the two halves, not in each one of them, so the disjunction is meaningless. So one must mention after the division of the limit to which arrival occurs so that arrival is not in the sum of the two halves, and after mentioning that, there is no need for the aforementioned disjunction.
His statement: '(So that between every two straight-line motions there is a rest)' – it was indicated in the beginning of the third purpose of this chapter that this ruling according to them is not specific to locative motions, as the description of motion here as straight suggests, but rather applies to others, and by this the author invalidated the occurrence of motion in the category of being affected, as passed there.

Commentary
This passage consists of four marginal notes that clarify points in the thirteenth purpose. The first note addresses the universality of the claim about rest, explaining that it is established by combining the issue of rest between motions with the finitude of dimensions, leading to the conclusion that dimensions and time-preserving motion are not instantaneous. The second note refines the proof about arrival: if arrival were temporal, it would occur over the sum of halves, making the original disjunction invalid; thus one must consider the division of the limit. The third note explains that the ruling about rest between straight motions is not limited to locative motions but applies to other categories as well, and that the author used this to refute motion in the category of being affected. The fourth note clarifies that Ahl al-Sunna also deny the necessity of rest, but their denial stems from lack of a complete proof for affirmation, unlike the Mu'tazila who have a positive proof.

Rectilinear Motion Ends in Rest
Proof that every rectilinear motion must end in rest due to finite dimensions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' proof that every rectilinear motion must end in rest because spatial dimensions are finite.

Core Idea
Every straight-line motion must pause because space has a limit.

The philosophers (falasifa) argue that any motion in a straight line cannot continue forever because the universe's dimensions are finite. This leads to a necessary moment of rest when the motion stops or changes direction. In this lesson, we examine their proof and its logical structure.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage uses a very compressed style.
2
Identify the key claim
The conclusion is that every rectilinear motion ends in rest. Find where this is stated.
3
Understand the reasoning
The argument from finite dimensions leads to three possibilities: stop, return, or turn, each requiring rest. Trace each possibility in the text.
4
Reflect on the assumption
The proof assumes that dimensions are finite. Consider why this is taken as given here.
This connects to broader discussions in kalam about the createdness of the world.
5
Consider the implication
This rest is a moment of no motion between two movements. Ask yourself: does this apply to all motions?
The philosophers argue it does for rectilinear motion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أن يسكن فيما بينهما (و) محصول ما ذكروه ( أنَّ كل حركة مستقيمة تنتهي ) البتة (إلى) سكون ) وذلك ) لأنها لا تذهب ) على الاستقامة ) إلى غير النهاية ) فإن الأبعاد ،متناهية، فإما أن تنقطع وهو ظاهر أو ترجع على سمتها أو تنعطف على سمت آخر وعلى التقديرين لا بد من سكون بين هاتين المستقيمتين فتكون الأولى منقطعة
That it comes to rest between them? (And) the gist of what they mentioned (is that every rectilinear motion ends necessarily in rest, and that because it does not go in a straight line to infinity, for dimensions are finite, so either it stops, which is apparent, or returns on its course, or turns to another course, and in all cases there must be rest between these two straight lines, so the first is interrupted.
Source pages p. 265

Commentary
This passage presents a proof by the philosophers (الحكماء) that any rectilinear motion must terminate in a state of rest between two segments of motion. The reasoning is based on the finiteness of spatial dimensions: since the universe is finite, a straight line cannot extend infinitely. Therefore, when an object moves in a straight line, it will eventually either stop (which is obvious), or it will reverse direction, or it will turn to another path. In each of the latter two cases, there must be a moment of rest between the two directions. This rest is necessary because the motion from one direction to another cannot occur without a pause, according to their assumptions. The argument concludes that the first motion is thus interrupted (منقطعة). The proof assumes the finiteness of dimensions, which is a separate established point.

Key Terms

الأبعاد متناهية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument claims that motion must stop entirely and never resume.
Correction: The argument only claims that between two rectilinear motions there is a moment of rest; the motion can continue after that rest in a different direction.

D) Because the object must stop before changing direction.

Key Takeaway
The proof shows that because space is finite, every straight-line motion must be interrupted by a pause when it changes direction or stops.

The Philosophers' Proof That Arrival Is Instantaneous
Objection from Plato and Mu'tazilites, then the philosophers' proof that arrival is instantaneous.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' argument that arrival at the endpoint must be instantaneous, and the objection from Plato and the Mu'tazilites that prompted it.

Core Idea
Arrival at the endpoint is instantaneous, not spread over time.

This passage presents an objection to the earlier proof that motion ends in rest. Plato and most Mu'tazilite theologians object, but the philosophers offer a proof that arrival is instantaneous. The argument uses a clever dilemma about the halves of time to show that temporal arrival leads to contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without overthinking. Then read the literal translation to get the gist.
Notice the names: Aflāṭūn (Plato) and the Mu'tazilites.
2
Identify the objection
The passage begins by stating that others (Plato and Mu'tazilites) objected. Understand that they object to the idea that motion necessarily ends in rest.
3
Understand the philosophers' claim
The affirmers (المثبتون) have two methods; the philosophers argue that arrival at the endpoint is instantaneous (āni).
The term 'āni' is key: it means instantaneous.
4
Follow the dilemma
The proof uses a reductio: if arrival were temporal, consider the first half of that time. Either arrival occurs in the first half (contradiction) or it doesn't (then it must occur in the second half, leading to the same problem).
This shows that arrival cannot take time; it must be instantaneous.
5
Reflect on the consequence
Consider why this matters: it preserves the idea that motion ends in a definite moment, not an extended interval.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنعه غيرهم كأفلاطون من الحكماء وأكثر المتكلمين من المعتزلة ( وأما المثبتون فلكل من الفريقين في إثباته طريق فقال الحكماء : الوصول إلى المنتهى آني ) إذ لو كان زمانياً ففي النصف الأول من ذلك الزمان إن حصل الوصول فذلك النصف هو زمان الوصول لا كله وهو خلاف المفروض، وإن لم يحصل كان حاصلاً في الثاني ويعود المحذور والأظهر أن يقال : الحد الذي هو منتهى المسافة الممتدة لا النصف
And others objected to him, like Plato among the philosophers and most of the theologians from the Mu'tazilites. And as for the affirmers, each of the two groups has a way of proving it. The philosophers said: Arrival at the endpoint is instantaneous, for if it were temporal, then in the first half of that time, if arrival occurs, then that half is the time of arrival, not the whole, which contradicts the assumption; and if it does not occur, then it occurs in the second half, and the same problem returns. And the more apparent is to say: The limit that is the endpoint of the extended distance, not the half.
Source pages p. 265

Commentary
This passage presents the objection to the earlier proof that motion necessarily ends in rest. The objectors include Plato (Aflāṭūn) among the philosophers and most of the Mu'tazilite theologians. Then the passage turns to the way of the affirmers (المثبتون), who have two methods. Here the philosophers argue that arrival at the endpoint must be instantaneous (āni). Their proof is by reductio: if arrival were temporal (taking time), consider the first half of that time. If arrival occurs in that half, then the time of arrival is only that half, not the whole period, contrary to the assumption that the whole period is the time of arrival. If arrival does not occur in the first half, then it must occur in the second half, and the same dilemma recurs. Therefore, arrival must be instantaneous, occurring in an indivisible moment. The commentator notes that a more apparent formulation is to say that the limit (the endpoint) is the end of the extended distance, not the half, which avoids the objection of the previous reasoning.

Key Terms
آني
زماني
وصول
المنتهى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Arrival takes time because motion takes time.
Correction: The philosophers argue that the moment of reaching the endpoint is instantaneous, even if the motion leading up to it takes time. The arrival at the point itself is a limit, not a duration.

Checkpoint

A) Because motion is continuous.
B) Because if it took time, there would be a contradiction about which half the arrival occurs in.
C) Because rest is instantaneous.
D) Because the endpoint is indivisible.

Key Takeaway
Arrival at the endpoint is instantaneous; this is proven by a dilemma about halves of time.

Instantaneous Return and Necessity of Rest
Philosophers prove instantaneousness of return and necessity of rest.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the philosophers argue that return motion is instantaneous and that a period of rest must exist between arrival and return to avoid the false composition of time from successive instants.

Core Idea
Return is instantaneous, and rest is necessary to prevent infinite succession of instants.

This lesson completes the philosophers' argument on motion and time. It builds on the instantaneousness of arrival to show that return is similarly instantaneous. However, since arrival and return cannot coexist, there must be a temporal interval of rest between them; otherwise, time would be composed of indivisible instants, a conclusion deemed false. Follow the logical steps carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal English translation. Let the structure of the argument emerge naturally.
The passage is dense; read slowly.
2
Understand why arrival is instantaneous
Identify the premise that the endpoint is indivisible. If arrival were temporal, the limit would be divisible due to gradual approach. Hence arrival must be instantaneous.
3
Notice the cause of motion (mīl) exists at the instant of arrival
The inclination that causes motion must exist at the same instant as the effect (arrival). This ties causality to instantaneousness.
4
Return is also instantaneous
The same reasoning applies to return; it too is instantaneous and its inclination exists at that instant.
Arrival and return are different motions that cannot coexist.
5
Grasp the consequence: no rest leads to succession of instants
If there were no time between arrival and return, instants would follow one another without duration, composing time from indivisible parts. This is considered false, so a rest period must exist.
The rest is the absence of motion, not a positive cause.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هو المرصد الرابع - المقصد الثاني عشر : قول الحكماء في علة البطء هي يكون منقسماً في ذلك الامتداد وإلا لم يكن بتمامه حداً، فالوصول إليه آني إذ لو كان زمانياً لكان ذلك الحد منقسماً لتعلق الوصول به شيئاً فشيئاً، ثم إن للوصول علة الميل فوجب أن تكون هذه العلة موجودة في آن الوصول لأن العلة الموجدة يجب وجودها حال وجود المعلول وهذا هو المراد بقوله : ( فكذلك الميل الموجب له ) أي أيضاً موجود في ذلك الآن مع حدوثه في آن ابتداء الحركة واستمراره إلى انتهائها ( والرجوع ) عن المنتهى أيضاً ( آني ( كالوصول ( فكذلك الميل الموجب له آني ) أي حادث في آن ) وأن الوصول غير أن الرجوع لامتناع اجتماعهما، فلو لم يكن بينهما زمان لزم تتالي الآنات وتركب الزمان منها ( وأنه باطل ) إذ يلزم حينئذ تركب الحركة من أجزاء لا تتجزأ، فيلزم تركب المسافة أيضاً منها ( فذلك الزمان لا حركة فيه ) لا إلى المنتهى، ولا عنه فهو (سكون أي زمان سكون )
It is the Fourth Observatory - the Twelfth Objective: The Philosophers' Statement on the Cause of Slowness. It becomes divisible in that extension, otherwise it would not be a limit in its entirety, so arrival at it is instantaneous, for if it were temporal, that limit would be divisible due to the connection of arrival to it gradually. Then, arrival has a cause of inclination (mīl), so it is necessary that this cause exist at the instant of arrival, because the efficient cause must exist at the time of the effect. This is what he meant by his saying: (Thus the inclination that necessitates it) i.e., it also exists at that instant, with its occurrence at the instant of the beginning of motion and its continuation until its end. And the return from the endpoint is also instantaneous like the arrival, so the inclination that necessitates it is also instantaneous, i.e., occurring in an instant. And arrival and return are different because they cannot coexist, so if there were no time between them, there would follow a succession of instants and composition of time from them, which is false, for then motion would be composed of indivisible parts, and consequently the distance would also be composed of them. So that time has no motion, neither toward the endpoint nor from it, so it is rest, i.e., a time of rest.
Source pages p. 265

Commentary
This passage completes the philosophers' argument. It begins by reiterating that arrival is instantaneous because the endpoint must be indivisible; if it were temporal, the limit would be divisible due to gradual arrival. Then it argues that the cause of motion (the inclination, mīl) must exist at the same instant as the effect. The return motion is also instantaneous, and its inclination is likewise instantaneous. Since arrival and return are mutually exclusive (they cannot coexist), if there were no time interval between them, then the instants would follow one another without any duration (تتالي الآنات), leading to the composition of time from these indivisible instants. This is considered false because it would imply that motion and distance are composed of indivisible parts (atoms of space-time). Therefore, there must be a stretch of time between arrival and return that contains no motion, i.e., a state of rest. This rest is not due to a positive cause but rather the absence of a cause of motion.

Key Terms

تتالي الآنات
تركب الزمان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The rest between arrival and return is a state of equilibrium or a positive cause.
Correction: The rest here is the absence of motion due to the impossibility of two opposite motions occurring simultaneously; it is not a positive state of balance but a necessary pause.

Checkpoint

A) Motion would be continuous.
B) Instants would follow successively, composing time.
C) Rest would be impossible.
D) The object would disappear.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers argue that return is instantaneous and rest is necessary to avoid the false composition of time from successive instants.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage shows that arrival and return cannot coexist; they require a rest. In your journey of repentance, do you allow a genuine pause for transformation before turning back to old habits? What one concrete change can you make to insert that 'rest' of sincere reflection?

?
If time were a succession of disconnected instants, your life would be a series of fragmented moments. Are you living as if each moment is isolated, rushing without the 'rest' of remembrance? Commit to a specific practice (e.g., pausing after salāh) that inserts a space of stillness between your daily activities.

The Three Senses of Idafa
Al-Jurjani defines al-ubuwa as relative and classifies three senses of idafa

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three distinct meanings of the term 'idafa' (relation) in kalam and how paternity serves as a paradigm of relational concepts.

Core Idea
Idafa has three meanings — the accident alone, the subject alone, or both together.

In this passage, al-Jurjani unpacks the concept of paternity (ubuwa) as a model for understanding relational categories. He shows that a relative notion like 'father' exists only in relation to another, and then clarifies that the word 'idafa' (relation) is used in three precise senses. This classification is essential for avoiding confusion when discussing attributes and relations in kalam.

Guided Path
1
Understand paternity as relative
Read the opening sentence: paternity is intelligible only in relation to another. Note that it has no reality independent of that relation.
This establishes the paradigm for all relational concepts.
2
Distinguish real vs. well-known relative
Identify the two categories: real relative (pure relation as accident) and well-known relative (the subject alone or composite of subject and accident).
The example: the father (subject) with paternity (accident) is well-known relative.
3
Memorize the three meanings of idafa
The word idafa applies to: (1) the accident alone, (2) the subject alone, and (3) the composite of both.
Context differentiates which meaning is intended.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقصد الأول : الأبوة هي المعقولة بالقياس إلى الغير ( الأبوة هي المعقولة بالقياس إلى الغير ولا حقيقة لها إلا ذلك ) أي ليس حقيقتها سوى أنها نسبة معقولة بالقياس إلى نسبة أخرى معقولة بالقياس إلى الأولى وحاصلها : النسبة المتكررة كما مر ( وهي الإضافة التي تعد من المقولات وتسمى مضافاً حقيقياً ويقال لذات الأب المعروضة لهذا العارض: إضافة). أيضاً ( وكذا) يقال : الإضافة للمعروض مع العارض وهذان يسميان مضافاً مشهورياً ) فلفظ الإضافة كلفظ المضاف يطلق على ثلاثة معان العارض وحده والمعروض وحده والمجموع المركب منهما قوله : (العارض وحده أي من غير اعتبار المعروض شطراً وكذا الثاني والقرينة مقابلتها للمجموع المركب منهما .
The first purpose: Paternity is the intelligible in relation to another. (Paternity is the intelligible in relation to another and it has no reality except that.) That is, its reality is nothing but a relation intelligible in relation to another relation intelligible in relation to the first, and its summary: the repeated relation as previously mentioned. (And it is the relation which is counted among the categories and is called real relative (mudaf haqiqi), and the essence of the father which is the subject of this accident is also called idafa.) Also it is said that idafa is used for the subject together with the accident, and these two are called 'well-known relative' (mudaf mashhuri). So the word idafa like the word mudaf is used for three meanings: the accident alone, the subject alone, and the composite of the two. His saying: 'the accident alone' meaning without considering the subject as a part, and similarly the second, and the context (qarina) is its contrast with the composite of the two.
Source pages p. 271

Commentary
This passage is a definition and classification. It defines paternity (al-ubuwa) as a paradigm of a relative notion. The author states that paternity is intelligible only in relation to another (the child) and has no reality beyond that relation. He then explains that its reality is a relation between two relations, often called a repeated relation. The passage then distinguishes between two types of relatives: real relative (al-mudaf al-haqiqi) and well-known relative (al-mudaf al-mashhuri). The real relative is the pure relation considered as an accident. The well-known relative has two senses: the subject alone (e.g., the father as possessing paternity) and the composite of subject and accident (the father and his paternity together). Consequently, the word 'idafa' (like 'mudaf') is used for three distinct meanings: the accident alone (the pure relation), the subject alone, and the composite of the two. The commentator's gloss clarifies that 'accident alone' means the relation considered without making the subject part of it. This classification is foundational for understanding the category of relation in kalam.

Key Terms
الأبوة
الإضافة
المضاف الحقيقي
المضاف المشهوري
العارض
المعروض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The term 'idafa' refers only to the relation itself, not to the subject or the composite.
Correction: The passage clarifies that idafa has three distinct meanings: the accident alone, the subject alone, and the composite of both, depending on context.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how many meanings does the word 'idafa' have?
A) One
B) Two
C) Three
D) Four

Key Takeaway
The word 'idafa' is used in three distinct senses in kalam: the relation alone, its subject, or the combination of both.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider a relationship you hold dear (e.g., father, mother, spouse). Do you see that relationship as a mere accident attached to you, or as part of your very reality? How does your answer change the way you act in that role?
?
If your relationships are not just accidents but 'real relatives' (mudaf haqiqi), then neglecting them neglects your own reality. Is there a relationship you have been treating as optional that is actually foundational to who you are? What one step will you take today to honor that relation?
?
The passage teaches that the same word 'idafa' can mean three different things. Are you similarly careless with words in your own life, using 'I love you' or 'I am responsible' without being precise about what you mean? Would your relationships be stronger if you spoke more exactly?

The Precise Meaning of Relative (Mudaf)
Al-Jurjani clarifies the definition of 'relative' and distinguishes it from necessary concomitants.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise meaning of 'relative' in Islamic philosophy and why it excludes necessary concomitants.

Core Idea
A relative's essence is incomplete without its correlate; necessary concomitants are not relatives.

Al-Jurjani presents a nuanced clarification of the definition of 'relative' (al-mudaf). The standard definition could be misinterpreted to include necessary attributes, so he distinguishes between them by focusing on how the essence depends on its correlate. This passage refines our understanding of relational concepts.

Guided Path
1
Read the core definition
Read the Arabic passage and its translation, focusing on the statement: 'the relative is that whose essence is intelligible in relation to another.'
Notice the cautionary note that this does not mean mere entailment.
2
Identify the two interpretations
The passage considers two possible meanings of 'from its reality it requires conceiving the other' and shows both have problems. Follow the commentator's reasoning.
The resolution involves the preposition 'bi' meaning accompaniment, not causation.
3
Distinguish real and well-known relative
Understand the difference between the real relative (inherently dependent) and the well-known relative (subject of relation). The passage clarifies that only the real relative fits the strict definition.
The well-known relative includes the subject alone, which is not dependent in itself.

فإنه ليس حد نفسه كذلك بل باعتبار عارضه . قوله : ( وإذا قيد ذلك الغير إلخ وإنما لم يقيد المصنف بذلك لأن مقصوده بيان معنى كونه معقولاً بالقياس إلى الغير. في قوله: (وهذان يسميان مضافاً مشهورياً ) قال في A note: His saying: 'The relative (al-mudaf) is that whose essence is intelligible in relation to another' is not intended to mean that conceiving the other is entailed [by it], for then the necessary concomitants (al-lawazim al-bayyina) would be such [i.e., they entail their subjects], so the essences with clear concomitants would enter into the definition of the relative. Rather, it is intended that from its reality it requires conceiving the other, so it cannot be conceived except by conceiving the other. That is, it is in itself such that its essence is not fully conceived except by conceiving something external to it. When that other is restricted to being a relation, the rest of the relations are excluded, and this statement includes both the real relative and the second type of the well-known relative. Complete. His saying: 'i.e., it is such that it entails etc.' So the text of the Matn is either based on deletion of the object of the relative, i.e., [it is] a necessary condition for the clear concomitants like the other in the necessity of their dependence [on the subject] due to the dependence of the subjects. His saying: 'From its reality [it requires] conceiving the other' contains a problem: if he means it is part of its reality, then the other is a part [such that] conceiving each of the two relatives depends on conceiving the other and its priority over it; if he means it arises from conceiving the reality of the other, then it is objected that the necessary concomitants of the essence are like that. Similarly in his saying 'it is not conceived except by conceiving' if the preposition 'bi' is taken as causal, priority is entailed; if taken as accompaniment, and the subjects [are considered] in relation to their clear concomitants, the expression is insufficient to express the intended meaning. The answer: The intended meaning [of 'bi'] is the completion of conceiving the other together with it because it is opposite to it, and the remaining [part of] his saying 'except by conceiving the other' is in the sense of 'with.' Its detail is in the Eastern Investigations (al-Mabahith al-Mashriqiyya) in agreement with al-Shifa: The meaning of 'the essence is predicated in relation to another' is that the essence requires in its conception the conception of something external. However, the subjects, when conceived, it is conceived with them that the essences of the subjects are not predicated in relation to the essences of the concomitants due to the necessity that the essence that is the subject or the necessary condition is independent in itself and prior in its essence to the concomitants, and it is impossible for the two relatives to be thus. Rather, the intelligible that needs the conception of another is not established in the mind nor in the external except due to the existence of that other opposite it. His saying: 'i.e., it is in itself etc.' Unlike the first type of the well-known relative, for it is not in itself like that, but rather by virtue of its accident. His saying: 'When that other is restricted etc.' And the author did not restrict it [in his definition] because his aim was to explain the meaning of being intelligible in relation to another. There follows: His saying: 'And these two are called well-known relative' in Sharh al-Maqasid: What occurred in al-Mawaqif that the subject alone is also called well-known relative is contrary to the well-known. Yes, it may be called mudaf in the sense that it is something that has idafa, in accordance with the linguistic convention. His saying: 'i.e., it is such that it entails etc.' The context requires that one say: the subjects with clear concomitants are like that. So the commentator pointed to the explanation mentioned by al-Abhari: that his word 'that' (dhalika) refers to the conception of the other, not to the composite. You may also interpret the author's expression as involving the deletion of the object of the relative, i.e., the things entailed by the concomitants. His saying: 'And when that other is restricted to being a relation' and the author did not restrict it here, relying on what preceded in the first observatory of this book.
Source pages p. 272

Commentary
This passage is a detailed analysis of the definition of a relative (al-mudaf). The commentator begins with a cautionary note: the well-known definition 'the relative is that whose essence is intelligible in relation to another' must not be misinterpreted as including necessary concomitants (al-lawazim al-bayyina), because those also entail conceiving something else (their subject), but the relative is different. The true meaning is that the relative's reality requires conceiving the other, such that its essence cannot be fully conceived without conceiving the other. When the other is restricted to being a relation (not any object), then the definition includes both the real relative and the second type of well-known relative (the composite). The commentator then examines the phrase 'from its reality it requires conceiving the other' and considers two possible interpretations: either the other is part of its reality, or it arises from conceiving the other. Both have problems: if part, it leads to circularity; if consequential, it collapses with necessary attributes. The solution is that the preposition 'bi' in 'except by conceiving' means accompaniment, not causation, and the relative's conception is completed by conceiving its opposite (muqabil). The commentator cites al-Mabahith al-Mashriqiyya to support this. He then distinguishes between the real relative (which is in itself dependent) and the first type of well-known relative (the subject alone, which is not dependent in itself but only by accident). Finally, he clarifies a point from Sharh al-Maqasid: calling the subject alone 'well-known relative' is not standard, but it can be called mudaf in a linguistic sense. The commentator also notes that the author did not restrict the definition to relation because his goal was to explain the meaning of 'intelligible in relation to another.'

المقابلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Necessary concomitants are relatives because they also require conceiving another.
Correction: A necessary concomitant can be conceived without its subject (though it entails it), whereas a relative's essence cannot be conceived at all without its correlate.

Checkpoint
Why does the definition of relative (al-mudaf) exclude necessary concomitants?
A) Because they are not relations
B) Because they do not require conceiving another
C) Because they entail their subjects but the relative requires conceiving its correlate for its very essence
D) Because they are not intelligible

Key Takeaway
The relative (mudaf) is defined by its essence requiring conception of another, unlike necessary attributes which merely entail their subjects.
